<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Shinto</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Religion from Japan</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Shinto_(disambiguation)" title="Shinto (disambiguation)">Shinto (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Gate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2803" data-file-width="4200" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/290px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/435px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/580px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Torii" title="Torii">torii</a> gateway to the <a href="/wiki/Itsukushima_Shrine" title="Itsukushima Shrine">Itsukushima Shrine</a> in Hiroshima Prefecture, Japan, one of the most famous examples of torii in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200270,_72_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200270,_72-1">[1]</a></sup> Torii mark the entrance to Shinto shrines and are recognizable symbols of the religion.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Shinto</b> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">神道</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Japanese" title="Romanization of Japanese">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Shintō</i></span>), or <b>Shintoism</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> that originated in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. Classified as an <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_religions" title="East Asian religions">East Asian religion</a> by <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">scholars of religion</a>, its practitioners often regard it as Japan's <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_religion" title="Indigenous religion">indigenous religion</a> and as a <a href="/wiki/Nature_religion" title="Nature religion">nature religion</a>. Scholars sometimes call its practitioners <i>Shintoists</i>, although adherents rarely use that term themselves. Shinto has no central authority in control and much diversity exists among practitioners.
</p><p>Shinto is <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> and revolves around the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">kami</a></i></span>, supernatural entities believed to inhabit all things. The link between the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and the natural world has led to Shinto being considered <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a>. The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are worshiped at <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kamidana" title="Kamidana">kamidana</a></i></span> household shrines, family shrines, and <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine"><i>jinja</i> public shrines</a>. The latter are staffed by priests, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kannushi" title="Kannushi">kannushi</a></i></span>, who oversee offerings of food and drink to the specific <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> enshrined at that location. This is done to cultivate harmony between humans and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and to solicit the latter's blessing. Other common rituals include the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kagura" title="Kagura">kagura</a></i></span> dances, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rites_of_passage" title="Rites of passage">rites of passage</a>, and seasonal festivals. Public shrines facilitate forms of <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> and supply religious objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a>, to the religion's adherents. Shinto places a major conceptual focus on ensuring purity, largely by cleaning practices such as ritual washing and bathing, especially before worship. Little emphasis is placed on specific moral codes or particular afterlife beliefs, although the dead are deemed capable of becoming <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>. The religion has no single creator or specific doctrine, and instead exists in a diverse range of local and regional forms.
</p><p>Although historians debate at what point it is suitable to refer to Shinto as a distinct religion, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> veneration has been traced back to Japan's <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a> (300 BCE to 300 CE), although it has been suggested that the concept may be older than that. <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Buddhism</a> entered Japan at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Kofun_period" title="Kofun period">Kofun period</a> (300 to 538 CE) and spread rapidly. <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">Religious syncretization</a> made <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship and Buddhism functionally inseparable, a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu-sh%C5%ABg%C5%8D" title="Shinbutsu-shūgō">shinbutsu-shūgō</a></i>. The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> came to be viewed as part of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology" title="Buddhist cosmology">Buddhist cosmology</a> and were increasingly depicted anthropomorphically. The earliest written tradition regarding <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship was recorded in the 8th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i>. In ensuing centuries, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinbutsu-shūgō</i></span> was adopted by Japan's Imperial household. During the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_era" title="Meiji era">Meiji era</a> (1868 to 1912), Japan's <a href="/wiki/Japanese_nationalism" title="Japanese nationalism">nationalist</a> leadership expelled Buddhist influence from <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship and formed <a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a>, which some historians regard as the origin of Shinto as a distinct religion. Shrines came under growing government influence, and citizens were encouraged to worship the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">emperor</a> as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>. With the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Empire" title="Japanese Empire">Japanese Empire</a> in the early 20th century, Shinto was exported to other areas of East Asia. Following Japan's defeat in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Shinto was formally <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">separated from the state</a>.
</p><p>Shinto is primarily found in Japan, where there are around 100,000 public shrines, although practitioners are also found abroad. Numerically, it is Japan's largest religion, the second being Buddhism. Most of the country's population takes part in both Shinto and Buddhist activities, especially festivals, reflecting a common view in <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Japan" title="Culture of Japan">Japanese culture</a> that the beliefs and practices of different religions need not be exclusive. Aspects of Shinto have been incorporated into various <a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">Japanese new religious movements</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YobitoTorii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/YobitoTorii.jpg/220px-YobitoTorii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/YobitoTorii.jpg/330px-YobitoTorii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/YobitoTorii.jpg/440px-YobitoTorii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YobitoTorii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A torii gateway to the Yobito Shrine (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Yobito-jinja</i></span>) in Abashiri City, Hokkaido</div></div></div>
<p>There is no universally agreed definition of Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viiiRots2015211_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viiiRots2015211-2">[2]</a></sup> However, the authors Joseph Cali and John Dougill stated that if there was "one single, broad definition of Shinto" that could be put forward, it would be that "Shinto is a belief in <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">kami</a></i></span>", the supernatural entities at the centre of the religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313-3">[3]</a></sup> The Japanologist <a href="/wiki/Helen_Hardacre" title="Helen Hardacre">Helen Hardacre</a> stated that "Shinto encompasses doctrines, institutions, ritual, and communal life based on <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171-4">[4]</a></sup> while the scholar of religion, Inoue Nobutaka, observed the term was "often used" in "reference to <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship and related theologies, rituals and practices".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInoue20031_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInoue20031-5">[5]</a></sup> Various scholars have referred to practitioners of Shinto as <i>Shintoists</i>, although this term has no direct translation in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviii_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviii-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have debated at what point in history it is legitimate to start talking about Shinto as a specific phenomenon. The scholar of religion, <a href="/wiki/Ninian_Smart" title="Ninian Smart">Ninian Smart</a>, suggested that one could "speak of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> religion of Japan, which lived symbiotically with organized Buddhism, and only later was institutionalized as Shinto."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmart1998135_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmart1998135-7">[7]</a></sup> While various institutions and practices now associated with Shinto existed in Japan by the 8th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718-8">[8]</a></sup> various scholars have argued that Shinto as a distinct religion was essentially "invented" during the 19th century, in Japan's <a href="/wiki/Meiji_era" title="Meiji era">Meiji era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107-9">[9]</a></sup> The scholar of religion, Brian Bocking, stressed that, especially when dealing with periods before the Meiji era, the term <i>Shinto</i> should "be approached with caution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997174_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997174-10">[10]</a></sup> Inoue Nobutaka stated that "Shinto cannot be considered as a single religious system that existed from the ancient to the modern period",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInoue20035_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInoue20035-11">[11]</a></sup> while the historian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuroda_Toshio" title="Kuroda Toshio">Kuroda Toshio</a>, noted that "before modern times Shinto did not exist as an independent religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19813_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19813-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Categorisation">Categorisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Categorisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many scholars describe Shinto as a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviiNelson199626_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviiNelson199626-13">[13]</a></sup> However, some practitioners prefer to view Shinto as a "way",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxivCaliDougill201313_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxivCaliDougill201313-14">[14]</a></sup> thus characterising it more as custom or <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> than religion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreen201069_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreen201069-15">[15]</a></sup> partly as a pretence for attempting to circumvent the modern Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Separation_of_religion_and_state" title="Separation of religion and state">separation of religion and state</a> and restore Shinto's historical links with the Japanese state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiv–xxv_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiv–xxv-16">[16]</a></sup> Moreover, religion as a concept arose in Europe and many of the connotations that the term has in <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western culture</a> "do not readily apply" to Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xix_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xix-17">[17]</a></sup> Unlike religions familiar in Western countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, Shinto has no single founder,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191Littleton20026Picken20111CaliDougill201313_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191Littleton20026Picken20111CaliDougill201313-18">[18]</a></sup> nor any single canonical text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191-19">[19]</a></sup> Western religions tend to stress exclusivity, but in Japan, it has long been considered acceptable to practice different religious traditions simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxx_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxx-20">[20]</a></sup> Japanese religion is therefore highly <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">pluralistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201148_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201148-21">[21]</a></sup> Shinto is often cited alongside <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> as one of Japan's two main religions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137-22">[22]</a></sup> and the two often differ in focus, with Buddhism emphasising the idea of transcending the cosmos, which it regards as being replete with suffering, while Shinto focuses on adapting to the pragmatic requirements of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199630Littleton200210_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199630Littleton200210-23">[23]</a></sup> Shinto has integrated elements from religious traditions imported into Japan from mainland Asia, such as Buddhism, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_fortune_telling" title="Chinese fortune telling">Chinese divination</a> practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987139CaliDougill201313_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987139CaliDougill201313-24">[24]</a></sup> It bears many similarities with other <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_religions" title="East Asian religions">East Asian religions</a>, in particular through its belief in many deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInoue20037_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInoue20037-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Some scholars suggest we talk about types of Shintō such as popular Shintō, folk Shintō, domestic Shintō, sectarian Shintō, imperial house Shintō, shrine Shintō, state Shintō, new Shintō religions, etc. rather than regard Shintō as a single entity. This approach can be helpful but begs the question of what is meant by 'Shintō' in each case, particularly since each category incorporates or has incorporated Buddhist, Confucian, Taoist, folk religious and other elements.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Scholar of religion Brian Bocking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997173–174_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997173–174-26">[26]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Scholars of religion have debated how to classify Shinto. Inoue considered it part of "the family of East-Asian religions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInoue200310_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInoue200310-27">[27]</a></sup> The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stuart_D._B._Picken" title="Stuart D. B. Picken">Stuart D. B. Picken</a> suggested that Shinto be classed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_religion" title="World religion">world religion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxv_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxv-28">[28]</a></sup> while the historian <a href="/wiki/H._Byron_Earhart" title="H. Byron Earhart">H. Byron Earhart</a> called it a "major religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431-29">[29]</a></sup> In the early 21st century it became increasingly common for practitioners to call Shinto a <a href="/wiki/Nature_religion" title="Nature religion">nature religion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015210_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015210-30">[30]</a></sup> It is also often described as an <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_religion" title="Indigenous religion">indigenous religion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19811Nelson19967Rots2015211_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19811Nelson19967Rots2015211-31">[31]</a></sup> although this generates debates over the various different definitions of "indigenous" in the Japanese context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19967_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19967-32">[32]</a></sup> The notion of Shinto as Japan's "indigenous religion" stemmed from the growth of modern nationalism in the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> to the Meiji era;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198119_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198119-33">[33]</a></sup> this view promoted the idea that Shinto's origins were prehistoric and that it represented something like the "underlying will of Japanese culture".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19811–2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19811–2-34">[34]</a></sup> The prominent Shinto theologian Sokyo Ono, for instance, said <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship was "an expression" of the Japanese "native racial faith which arose in the mystic days of remote antiquity" and that it was "as indigenous as the people that brought the Japanese nation into existence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xviii_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xviii-35">[35]</a></sup> Many scholars regard this classification as inaccurate. Earhart noted that Shinto, in having absorbed much Chinese and Buddhist influence, was "too complex to be labelled simply [as an] indigenous religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is substantial local variation in how Shinto is practiced;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979215_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979215-36">[36]</a></sup> the anthropologist John K. Nelson noted it was "not a unified, monolithic entity that has a single center and system all its own".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19967_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19967-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shinto_sects_and_schools" title="Shinto sects and schools">Different types of Shinto</a> have been identified. "Shrine Shinto" refers to the practices centred around shrines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192Nelson19967_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192Nelson19967-37">[37]</a></sup> and "Domestic Shinto" to the ways in which <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are venerated in the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192-38">[38]</a></sup> Some scholars have used the term "Folk Shinto" to designate localised Shinto practices,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314-39">[39]</a></sup> or practices outside of an institutionalised setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19967_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19967-32">[32]</a></sup> In various eras of the past, there was also a "<a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a>", in which Shinto beliefs and practices were closely interlinked with the Japanese state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192Nelson19967_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979192Nelson19967-37">[37]</a></sup> In representing "a portmanteau term" for many varied traditions across Japan, the term "Shinto" is similar to the term "<a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>", used to describe varied traditions across South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viii_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viii-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2700" data-file-width="4050" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg/220px-Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg/330px-Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg/440px-Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Takachiho-gawara_Kirishima_City_Kagoshima_Pref02n4050.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A torii gate at the <a href="/wiki/Takachiho-gawara" title="Takachiho-gawara">Takachiho-gawara</a> shrine near <a href="/wiki/Kirishima,_Kagoshima" title="Kirishima, Kagoshima">Kirishima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kagoshima_Prefecture" title="Kagoshima Prefecture">Kagoshima Prefecture</a>, which is associated with the mythological tale of <a href="/wiki/Ninigi-no-Mikoto" title="Ninigi-no-Mikoto">Ninigi-no-Mikoto</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Tenson_k%C5%8Drin" title="Tenson kōrin">descent to earth</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>Shinto</i> is often translated into English as "the way of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979193Kitagawa1987139Bocking1997173Nelson200014Earhart20042Picken20119_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979193Kitagawa1987139Bocking1997173Nelson200014Earhart20042Picken20119-41">[41]</a></sup> although its meaning has varied throughout Japanese history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19814Bocking1997viii,_173_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19814Bocking1997viii,_173-42">[42]</a></sup> Other terms are sometimes used synonymously with "Shinto"; these include <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami no michi</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">神の道</span></span>, "the way of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>"), <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kannagara no michi</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">神ながらの道</span></span>, also written <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">随神の道</span></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">惟神の道</span></span>, "the way of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> from time immemorial"), <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kodō</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">古道</span></span>, "the ancient way"), <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Daidō</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">大道</span></span>, "the great way"), and <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Teidō</i></span></i> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">帝道</span></span>, "the imperial way").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxivPicken201164_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxivPicken201164-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Shinto</i> derives from the combination of two Chinese characters: <i><a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">shen</a></i> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1094882035">.mw-parser-output .script-Cprt{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Cypriot",Code2001}.mw-parser-output .script-Hano{font-size:125%;font-family:"Noto Sans Hanunoo",FreeSerif,Quivira}.mw-parser-output .script-Latf,.mw-parser-output .script-de-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Breitkopf Fraktur",UnifrakturCook,UniFrakturMaguntia,MarsFraktur,"MarsFraktur OT",KochFraktur,"KochFraktur OT",OffenbacherSchwabOT,"LOB.AlteSchwabacher","LOV.AlteSchwabacher","LOB.AtlantisFraktur","LOV.AtlantisFraktur","LOB.BreitkopfFraktur","LOV.BreitkopfFraktur","LOB.FetteFraktur","LOV.FetteFraktur","LOB.Fraktur3","LOV.Fraktur3","LOB.RochFraktur","LOV.RochFraktur","LOB.PostFraktur","LOV.PostFraktur","LOB.RuelhscheFraktur","LOV.RuelhscheFraktur","LOB.RungholtFraktur","LOV.RungholtFraktur","LOB.TheuerbankFraktur","LOV.TheuerbankFraktur","LOB.VinetaFraktur","LOV.VinetaFraktur","LOB.WalbaumFraktur","LOV.WalbaumFraktur","LOB.WeberMainzerFraktur","LOV.WeberMainzerFraktur","LOB.WieynckFraktur","LOV.WieynckFraktur","LOB.ZentenarFraktur","LOV.ZentenarFraktur"}.mw-parser-output .script-en-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:Cankama,"Old English Text MT","Textura Libera","Textura Libera Tenuis",London}.mw-parser-output .script-it-Latf{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Rotunda Pommerania",Rotunda,"Typographer Rotunda"}.mw-parser-output .script-Lina{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Noto Sans Linear A"}.mw-parser-output .script-Linb{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Noto Sans Linear B"}.mw-parser-output .script-Ugar{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Ugaritic",Aegean}.mw-parser-output .script-Xpeo{font-size:1.25em;font-family:"Segoe UI Historic","Noto Sans Old Persian",Artaxerxes,Xerxes,Aegean}</style><span class="Unicode">神</span>), which means "spirit," and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dao" title="Dao">dao</a></i> (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1094882035" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="Unicode">道</span>), which means "way", "road" or "path".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987139Littleton20026Picken20119_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987139Littleton20026Picken20119-44">[44]</a></sup> "Shendao" (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">神道</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shéndào</span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> 'the Way of the Gods') was a term already used in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yijing" title="Yijing">Yijing</a></i> referring to the divine order of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Around the time of the spread of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han period</a> (206 BC – 220 AD), it was used to distinguish <a href="/wiki/Chinese_folk_religion" title="Chinese folk religion">indigenous Chinese religions</a> from the imported religion. <a href="/wiki/Ge_Hong" title="Ge Hong">Ge Hong</a> used it in his <i><a href="/wiki/Baopuzi" title="Baopuzi">Baopuzi</a></i> as a synonym for <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> term <i>Shendao</i> was originally adopted into Japanese as <i>Jindō</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002243_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002243-47">[47]</a></sup> this was possibly first used as a Buddhist term to refer to non-Buddhist deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002256_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002256-48">[48]</a></sup> Among the earliest known appearances of the term <i>Shinto</i> in Japan is in the 8th-century text, <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002236Hardacre201741_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeuwen2002236Hardacre201741-49">[49]</a></sup> Here, it may be a generic term for popular belief,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19814–5Teeuwen2002237_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19814–5Teeuwen2002237-50">[50]</a></sup> or alternatively reference Taoism, as many Taoist practices had recently been imported from mainland Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19816Teeuwen2002237Hardacre201742_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19816Teeuwen2002237Hardacre201742-51">[51]</a></sup> In these early Japanese uses, the word <i>Shinto</i> did not apply to a distinct religious tradition nor to anything uniquely Japanese;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19817_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19817-52">[52]</a></sup> the 11th century <i><a href="/wiki/Konjaku_Monogatarish%C5%AB" title="Konjaku Monogatarishū">Konjaku monogatarishui</a></i> for instance refers to a woman in China practicing <i>Shinto</i>, and also to people in India worshipping <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, indicating these terms were being used to describe religions outside Japan itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819–10_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819–10-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In medieval Japan, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>-worship was generally seen as being part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Buddhism" title="Japanese Buddhism">Japanese Buddhism</a>, with the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> themselves often interpreted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhas" title="Buddhas">Buddhas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198111,_12_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198111,_12-54">[54]</a></sup> At this point, the term <i>Shinto</i> increasingly referred to "the authority, power, or activity of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, being a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, or, in short, the state or attributes of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110-55">[55]</a></sup> It appears in this form in texts such as <i><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nakatomi no harai kunge</i></span></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Shint%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Shintōshū">Shintōshū</a></i> tales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110-55">[55]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Nippo_Jisho" title="Nippo Jisho">Japanese Portuguese Dictionary</a></i> of 1603, <i>Shinto</i> is defined as referring to "<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> or matters pertaining to <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110–11_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198110–11-56">[56]</a></sup> The term <i>Shinto</i> became common in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201742_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201742-57">[57]</a></sup> During the late Edo period, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kokugaku" title="Kokugaku">kokugaku</a></i></span> scholars began using the term <i>Shinto</i> to describe what they believed was an ancient, enduring and indigenous Japanese tradition that predated Buddhism; they argued that <i>Shinto</i> should be used to distinguish <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship from traditions like Buddhism, Taoism, and Confucianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda198119Bocking1997174_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda198119Bocking1997174-58">[58]</a></sup> This use of the term <i>Shinto</i> became increasingly popular from the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997174_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997174-10">[10]</a></sup> The term <i>Shinto</i> has been commonly used only since the early 20th century, when it superseded the term <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">taikyō</i></span> ('great religion') as the name for the Japanese state religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viii_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997viii-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kami"><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Kami">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">Kami</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2621" data-file-width="1841" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg/200px-A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg/300px-A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg/400px-A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_man_confronted_with_an_apparition_of_the_Fox_goddess.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An artistic depiction by <a href="/wiki/Utagawa_Kuniyoshi" title="Utagawa Kuniyoshi">Utagawa Kuniyoshi</a> of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> Inari appearing to a man</div></div></div>
<p>Shinto is <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a>, involving the veneration of many deities known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200223CaliDougill201313_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200223CaliDougill201313-59">[59]</a></sup> or sometimes as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">jingi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199770Hardacre201731_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199770Hardacre201731-60">[60]</a></sup> As is often the case in Japanese, no distinction is made here between singular and plural, and hence the term <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> refers both to individual <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and the collective group of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535CaliDougill201313_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535CaliDougill201313-61">[61]</a></sup> Although lacking a direct English translation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048-62">[62]</a></sup> the term <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> has sometimes been rendered as "god" or "spirit";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20042CaliDougill201313_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20042CaliDougill201313-63">[63]</a></sup> the historian of religion <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kitagawa" title="Joseph Kitagawa">Joseph Kitagawa</a> stated that these English translations were "quite unsatisfactory and misleading",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198736_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198736-64">[64]</a></sup> and various scholars urge against translating <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> into English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979194Bocking199784_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979194Bocking199784-65">[65]</a></sup> In Japanese, it is often said that there are <a href="/wiki/Indefinite_and_fictitious_numbers#Specific_values_used_as_indefinite" title="Indefinite and fictitious numbers">eight million</a> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, a term which connotes an infinite number,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199629Littleton200224_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199629Littleton200224-66">[66]</a></sup> and Shinto practitioners believe that they are present everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171-4">[4]</a></sup> They are not regarded as <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscient</a>, or necessarily <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535Hardacre201752_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535Hardacre201752-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> is "conceptually fluid",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535-68">[68]</a></sup> and "vague and imprecise".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979194_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979194-69">[69]</a></sup> In Japanese it is often applied to the power of phenomena that inspire a sense of wonder and awe in the beholder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiBoydWilliams200535_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiBoydWilliams200535-70">[70]</a></sup> Kitagawa referred to this as "the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> nature", stating that he thought it "somewhat analogous" to the Western ideas of the <a href="/wiki/Numinous" title="Numinous">numinous</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sacred" title="Sacred">sacred</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198736_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198736-64">[64]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span> are seen to inhabit both the living and the dead, organic and inorganic matter, and natural disasters like earthquakes, droughts, and plagues;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313-3">[3]</a></sup> their presence is seen in natural forces such as the wind, rain, fire, and sunshine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314-39">[39]</a></sup> Accordingly, Nelson commented that Shinto regards "the <i>actual phenomena</i> of the world itself" as being "divine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199626_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199626-71">[71]</a></sup> The Shinto understanding of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> has also been characterised as being <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19967Picken201140CaliDougill201313_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19967Picken201140CaliDougill201313-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Japan, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> have been venerated since prehistory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171-4">[4]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a> were regarded as being formless and invisible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719-73">[73]</a></sup> It was only under the influence of Buddhism that they were depicted anthropomorphically;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997180Hardacre20171_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997180Hardacre20171-74">[74]</a></sup> statues of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinzo</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997180_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997180-75">[75]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span> are usually associated with a specific place, often one that is noted as a prominent feature in the landscape such as a waterfall, mountain, large rock, or distinctive tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200275CaliDougill201314_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200275CaliDougill201314-76">[76]</a></sup> Physical objects or places in which the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are believed to have a presence are termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shintai" title="Shintai">shintai</a></i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997172_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997172-77">[77]</a></sup> objects inhabited by the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> that are placed in the shrine are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">go-shintai</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Nelson1996144_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Nelson1996144-78">[78]</a></sup> Objects commonly chosen for this purpose include mirrors, swords, stones, beads, and inscribed tablets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Earhart200436–37_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Earhart200436–37-79">[79]</a></sup> These <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">go-shintai</i></span> are concealed from the view of visitors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Picken201144_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202Picken201144-80">[80]</a></sup> and may be hidden inside boxes so that even the priests do not know what they look like.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997172_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997172-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span> are believed to be capable of both benevolent and destructive deeds;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199627CaliDougill201313_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199627CaliDougill201313-81">[81]</a></sup> if warnings about good conduct are ignored, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> can mete out punishment called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinbatsu</i></span>, often taking the form of illness or sudden death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997164_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997164-82">[82]</a></sup> Some <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, referred to as the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">magatsuhi-no-kami</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">araburu kami</i></span>, are regarded as being essentially malevolent and destructive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997114Picken201142_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997114Picken201142-83">[83]</a></sup> Offerings and prayers are given to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to gain their blessings and to dissuade them from engaging in destructive actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313-3">[3]</a></sup> Shinto seeks to cultivate and ensure a harmonious relationship between humans and the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and thus with the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20047–8_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20047–8-84">[84]</a></sup> More localised <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> may be subject to feelings of intimacy and familiarity from members of the local community that are not directed towards more widespread <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> like Amaterasu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199633_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199633-85">[85]</a></sup> The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> of a particular community is referred to it as their <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ujigami</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997214–215Littleton200224_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997214–215Littleton200224-86">[86]</a></sup> while that of a particular house is the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yashikigami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997222_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997222-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span> are not deemed <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysically</a> different from humanity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200535-68">[68]</a></sup> with it being possible for humans to become <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048-62">[62]</a></sup> Dead humans are sometimes venerated as kami, being regarded as protector or ancestral figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200227CaliDougill201313Hardacre20171_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200227CaliDougill201313Hardacre20171-88">[88]</a></sup> One of the most prominent examples is that of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_%C5%8Cjin" title="Emperor Ōjin">Emperor Ōjin</a>, who on his death was enshrined as the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> <a href="/wiki/Hachiman" title="Hachiman">Hachiman</a>, believed to be a protector of Japan and a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200231–32CaliDougill201314_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200231–32CaliDougill201314-89">[89]</a></sup> In Japanese culture, ancestors can be viewed as a form of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200410_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200410-90">[90]</a></sup> In Western Japan, the term <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jigami" title="Jigami">jigami</a></i></span> is used to describe the enshrined <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> of a village founder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199769_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199769-91">[91]</a></sup> In some cases, living human beings were also viewed as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313-3">[3]</a></sup> these were called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">akitsumi kami</i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201135–36_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201135–36-92">[92]</a></sup> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">arahito-gami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201142_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201142-93">[93]</a></sup> In the State Shinto system of the Meiji era, the emperor of Japan was declared to be a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048-62">[62]</a></sup> while several Shinto sects have also viewed their leaders as living <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20048-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3632" data-file-width="4842" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg/220px-Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg/330px-Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg/440px-Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hokora_in_Takeo_no_Okusu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 3000 year old sacred tree (<a href="/wiki/Shintai" title="Shintai">shintai</a>) of Takeo Shrine</div></div></div>
<p>Although some <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are venerated only in a single location, others have shrines devoted to them across many areas of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup> Hachiman for instance has around 25,000 shrines dedicated to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314-39">[39]</a></sup> The act of establishing a new shrine to a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> who already has one is called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Bunrei" title="Bunrei">bunrei</a></i></span> ("dividing the spirit").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Picken201157CaliDougill201315_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Picken201157CaliDougill201315-95">[95]</a></sup> As part of this, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> is invited to enter a new place, where it can be venerated, with the instalment ceremony known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanjo" title="Kanjo">kanjo</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup> The new, subsidiary shrine is known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">bunsha</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Picken201158_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Picken201158-96">[96]</a></sup> Individual <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are not believed to have their power diminished by their residence in multiple locations, and there is no limit on the number of places a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> can be enshrined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup> In some periods, fees were charged for the right to enshrine a particular <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> in a new place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup> Shrines are not necessarily always designed as permanent structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre20171-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many kami are believed to have messengers, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami no tsukai</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tsuka washime</i></span>, and these are generally depicted as taking animal forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup> The messenger of Inari, for example, is depicted as a fox (<i><a href="/wiki/Kitsune" title="Kitsune">kitsune</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201140CaliDougill201315_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201140CaliDougill201315-97">[97]</a></sup> while the messenger of Hachiman is a dove.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315_94-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201315-94">[94]</a></sup>
Shinto cosmology also includes <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bakemono" title="Bakemono">bakemono</a></i></span>, spirits who cause malevolent acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19978_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19978-98">[98]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Bakemono</i></span> include <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Oni" title="Oni">oni</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tengu" title="Tengu">tengu</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kappa_(folklore)" title="Kappa (folklore)">kappa</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Mononoke" title="Mononoke">mononoke</a></i></span>, and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yama-uba" title="Yama-uba">yamanba</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19978_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19978-98">[98]</a></sup> Japanese folklore also incorporates belief in the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">goryō</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">onryō</i></span>, unquiet or vengeful spirits, particularly of those who have died violently and without appropriate funerary rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199737_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199737-99">[99]</a></sup> These are believed to inflict suffering on the living, meaning that they must be pacified, usually through Buddhist rites but sometimes through enshrining them as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199737_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199737-99">[99]</a></sup> Other Japanese supernatural figures include the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Bake-danuki" title="Bake-danuki">tanuki</a></i></span>, animal like creatures who can take human form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997200_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997200-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmogony">Cosmogony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cosmogony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amenominakanushi" title="Amenominakanushi">Amenominakanushi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_creation_myth" title="Japanese creation myth">Japanese creation myth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1307" data-file-width="592" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg/150px-Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg/225px-Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg/300px-Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kobayashi_Izanami_and_Izanagi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Izanami" title="Izanami">Izanami</a>-no-Mikoto and <a href="/wiki/Izanagi" title="Izanagi">Izanagi</a>-no-Mikoto, by Kobayashi Eitaku, late 19th century</div></div></div>
<p>The origin of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and of Japan itself are recounted in two eighth-century texts, <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i>Nihon Shoki</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979195Kitagawa1987142Littleton200223Earhart200432CaliDougill201318_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979195Kitagawa1987142Littleton200223Earhart200432CaliDougill201318-101">[101]</a></sup> although the accounts they provide differ in part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201748–49_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201748–49-102">[102]</a></sup> Drawing heavily on Chinese influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979195Kitagawa1987142Littleton200237Earhart200433_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979195Kitagawa1987142Littleton200237Earhart200433-103">[103]</a></sup> these texts were commissioned by ruling elites to legitimize and consolidate their rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200433–34CaliDougill201318–19_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200433–34CaliDougill201318–19-104">[104]</a></sup> Although never of great importance to Japanese religious life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200433_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200433-105">[105]</a></sup> in the early 20th century the government proclaimed that their accounts were factual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201319_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201319-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Kojiki</i> recounts that the universe started with <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ame-tsuchi</i></span>, the separation of light and pure elements (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ame</i></span>, "heaven") from heavy elements (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tsuchi</i></span>, "earth").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19975Picken201138CaliDougill201319_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19975Picken201138CaliDougill201319-107">[107]</a></sup> Three kami then appeared: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amenominakanushi" title="Amenominakanushi">Amenominakanushi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Takamimusubi" title="Takamimusubi">Takamimusuhi no Mikoto</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kamimusubi" title="Kamimusubi">Kamimusuhi no Mikoto</a>. Other kami followed, including a brother and sister, <a href="/wiki/Izanagi" title="Izanagi">Izanagi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Izanami" title="Izanami">Izanami</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201319Hardacre201748_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201319Hardacre201748-108">[108]</a></sup> The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> instructed Izanagi and Izanami to create land on earth. To this end, the siblings stirred the briny sea with a jewelled spear, from which <a href="/wiki/Onogoro_Island" title="Onogoro Island">Onogoro Island</a> was formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143CaliDougill201319–20Hardacre201749_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143CaliDougill201319–20Hardacre201749-109">[109]</a></sup> Izanagi and Izanami then descended to Earth, where the latter gave birth to further <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>. One of these was a fire <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, whose birth killed Izanami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143CaliDougill201320Hardacre201750_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143CaliDougill201320Hardacre201750-110">[110]</a></sup> Izanagi then descended to the netherworld (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yomi</i></span>) to retrieve his sister, but there he saw her body putrefying. Embarrassed to be seen in this state, she chased him out of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yomi</i></span>, and he closed its entrance with a boulder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143Bocking199767CaliDougill201320Hardacre201750_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143Bocking199767CaliDougill201320Hardacre201750-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Izanagi bathed in the sea to rid himself from the pollution brought about by witnessing Izanami's putrefaction. Through this act, further kami emerged from his body: <a href="/wiki/Amaterasu" title="Amaterasu">Amaterasu</a> (the sun <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>) was born from his left eye, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tsukuyomi" title="Tsukuyomi">Tsukuyomi</a> (the moon <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>) from his right eye, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Susanoo" title="Susanoo">Susanoo</a> (the storm <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>) from his nose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979196Kitagawa1987143Bocking199767CaliDougill201320Hardacre201753_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979196Kitagawa1987143Bocking199767CaliDougill201320Hardacre201753-112">[112]</a></sup> Susanoo behaved in a destructive manner, and to escape him Amaterasu hid herself within a cave, plunging the earth into darkness. The other <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> eventually succeeded in coaxing her out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979196–197Kitagawa1987144Bocking19973CaliDougill201321Hardacre201753–54_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979196–197Kitagawa1987144Bocking19973CaliDougill201321Hardacre201753–54-113">[113]</a></sup> Susanoo was then banished to earth, where he married and had children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201322Hardacre201754_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201322Hardacre201754-114">[114]</a></sup> According to the <i>Kojiki</i>, Amaterasu then sent her grandson, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ninigi" title="Ninigi">Ninigi</a>, to rule Japan, giving him curved beads, a mirror, and a sword: the symbols of Japanese imperial authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987144Hardacre201757_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987144Hardacre201757-115">[115]</a></sup> Amaterasu remains probably Japan's most venerated <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200298_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200298-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology_and_afterlife">Cosmology and afterlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cosmology and afterlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Shinto, the creative principle permeating all life is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">musubi</i></span>, and is associated with its own <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997129BoydWilliams200534_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997129BoydWilliams200534-117">[117]</a></sup> Within traditional Japanese thought, there is no concept of an overarching duality between good and evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200226Picken201136_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200226Picken201136-118">[118]</a></sup> The concept of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">aki</i></span> encompasses misfortune, unhappiness, and disaster, although it does not correspond precisely with the Western concept of evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201136_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201136-119">[119]</a></sup> There is no <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatology</a> in Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201171_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201171-120">[120]</a></sup> 
Texts such as the <i>Kojiki</i> and <i>Nihon Shoki</i> portray multiple realms in Shinto cosmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoerner1977153–154_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoerner1977153–154-121">[121]</a></sup> These present a universe divided into three parts: the Plain of High Heaven (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Takama-no-hara</i></span>), where the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> live; the Phenomenal or Manifested World (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Utsushi-yo</i></span>), where humans dwell; and the Nether World (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Yomotsu-kuni</i></span>), where unclean spirits reside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143Bocking1997216_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143Bocking1997216-122">[122]</a></sup> The mythological texts nevertheless do not draw firm demarcations between these realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987143-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shinto includes belief in a human spirit or soul, called the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mitama</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tamashii</i></span>, which contains four aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201775_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201775-124">[124]</a></sup> Although indigenous ideas about an afterlife were probably well-developed prior to the arrival of Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200290_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200290-125">[125]</a></sup> contemporary Japanese people often adopt Buddhist concepts about an afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200289_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200289-126">[126]</a></sup> Modern Shinto places greater emphasis on this life than on any afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoerner1977153Littleton200290_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoerner1977153Littleton200290-127">[127]</a></sup> Mythological stories like the <i>Kojiki</i> describe <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yomi</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yomi-no-kuni</i></span> as a realm of the dead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200290Picken201171_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200290Picken201171-128">[128]</a></sup> although this plays no role in modern Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200290_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200290-125">[125]</a></sup> Modern Shinto ideas about the afterlife largely revolve around the idea that the spirit survives bodily death and continues to assist the living. After 33 years, it then becomes part of the family <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200289–91_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200289–91-129">[129]</a></sup> These ancestral spirits are sometimes thought to reside in the mountains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200291Picken201139_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200291Picken201139-130">[130]</a></sup> from where they descend to take part in agricultural events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201139_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201139-131">[131]</a></sup> Shinto's afterlife beliefs also include the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">obake</i></span>, restless spirits who died in bad circumstances and often seek revenge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200292_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200292-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purity_and_impurity">Purity and impurity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Purity and impurity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A key theme in Shinto is the avoidance of <i><a href="/wiki/Kegare" title="Kegare">kegare</a></i> ("pollution" or "impurity"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199793CaliDougill201320_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199793CaliDougill201320-133">[133]</a></sup> while ensuring <i><a href="/wiki/Harae" title="Harae">harae</a></i> ("purity").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996101Bocking199745CaliDougill201321_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996101Bocking199745CaliDougill201321-134">[134]</a></sup> In Japanese thought, humans are seen as fundamentally pure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201145_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201145-135">[135]</a></sup> <i>Kegare</i> is therefore seen as being a temporary condition that can be corrected through achieving <i>harae</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199793_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199793-136">[136]</a></sup> Rites of purification are conducted so as to restore an individual to "spiritual" health and render them useful to society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996102_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996102-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2916" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg/220px-Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg/330px-Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg/440px-Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karasuzumo_purification_ritual.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shinto purification rite after a ceremonial children's <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> tournament at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamigamo_Jinja" title="Kamigamo Jinja">Kamigamo Jinja</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a></div></div></div>
<p>This notion of purity is present in many facets of Japanese culture, such as the focus it places on bathing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199638_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199638-138">[138]</a></sup> Purification is for instance regarded as important in preparation for the planting season,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199663_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199663-139">[139]</a></sup> while performers of <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a> theatre undergo a purification rite before they carry out their performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken20117_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken20117-140">[140]</a></sup> Among the things regarded as particular pollutants in Shinto are death, disease, witchcraft, the flaying alive of an animal, incest, bestiality, excrement, and blood associated with either menstruation or childbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979206Nelson1996104_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979206Nelson1996104-141">[141]</a></sup> To avoid <i>kegare</i>, priests and other practitioners may engage in abstinence and avoid various activities prior to a festival or ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199793_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199793-136">[136]</a></sup>
