Various words, termed <i>imi-kotoba</i>, are also regarded as taboo, and people avoid speaking them when at a shrine; these include <i>shi</i> (death), <i>byō</i> (illness), and <i>shishi</i> (meat).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199758_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199758-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>A purification ceremony known as <i>misogi</i> involves the use of fresh water, salt water, or salt to remove <i>kegare</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124-143">[143]</a></sup> Full immersion in the sea is often regarded as the most ancient and efficacious form of purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996140_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996140-144">[144]</a></sup> This act links with the mythological tale in which Izanagi immersed himself in the sea to purify himself after discovering his deceased wife; it was from this act that other kami sprang from his body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996141Bocking1997124_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996141Bocking1997124-145">[145]</a></sup> An alternative is immersion beneath a waterfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124Picken201145_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124Picken201145-146">[146]</a></sup> Salt is often regarded as a purifying substance;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996141Earhart200411_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996141Earhart200411-147">[147]</a></sup> some Shinto practitioners will for instance sprinkle salt on themselves after a funeral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996141–142Picken201170_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996141–142Picken201170-148">[148]</a></sup> while those running restaurants may put a small pile of salt outside before business commences each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken20116_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken20116-149">[149]</a></sup> Fire, also, is perceived as a source of purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200411_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200411-150">[150]</a></sup> The <i>yaku-barai</i> is a form of harae designed to prevent misfortune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997219_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997219-151">[151]</a></sup> while the <i>oharae</i>, or "ceremony of great purification", is often used for end-of-year purification rites, and is conducted twice a year at many shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997136_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997136-152">[152]</a></sup> Before the Meiji period, rites of purification were generally performed by <a href="/wiki/Onmy%C5%8Dji" title="Onmyōji">onmyōji</a>, a type of diviner whose practices derived from the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> philosophy. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201012_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201012-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Kannagara.2C_morality.2C_and_ethics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kannagara,_morality,_and_ethics"><i>Kannagara</i>, morality, and ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kannagara, morality, and ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Shinto, <i>kannagara</i> ("way of the kami") describes the law of the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">natural order</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiii_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiii-154">[154]</a></sup> with <i>wa</i> ("benign harmony") being inherent in all things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200258_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200258-155">[155]</a></sup> Disrupting <i>wa</i> is deemed bad, contributing to it is thought good;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200258,_61_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200258,_61-156">[156]</a></sup> as such, subordination of the individual to the larger social unit has long been a characteristic of the religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200211,_57_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200211,_57-157">[157]</a></sup> Shinto incorporates morality tales and myths but no overarching, codified ethical doctrine;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313-3">[3]</a></sup> Offner noted that Shinto specified no "unified, systematized code of behaviour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979191-19">[19]</a></sup> Its views of <i>kannagara</i> influence certain ethical views, focused on sincerity (<i>makoto</i>) and honesty (<i>tadashii</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiii_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxiii-154">[154]</a></sup> <i>Makoto</i> is regarded as a cardinal virtue in Japanese religion more broadly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997115_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997115-158">[158]</a></sup> Shinto sometimes includes reference to four virtues known as the <i>akaki kiyoki kokoro</i> or <i>sei-mei-shin</i>, meaning "purity and cheerfulness of heart", which are linked to the state of harae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997157Picken201134_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997157Picken201134-159">[159]</a></sup> Offner believed that in Shinto, ideas about goodness linked to "that which possesses, or relates to, beauty, brightness, excellence, good fortune, nobility, purity, suitability, harmony, conformity, [and] productivity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979198_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979198-160">[160]</a></sup> <i>Shojiki</i> is regarded as a virtue, encompassing honesty, uprightness, veracity, and frankness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182-161">[161]</a></sup> Shinto's flexibility regarding morality and ethics has been a source of frequent criticism, especially from those arguing that the religion can readily become a pawn for those wishing to use it to legitimise their authority and power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996198_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996198-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout Japanese history, the notion of <i>saisei-itchi</i>, or the union of religious authority and political authority, has long been prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xvii_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xvii-163">[163]</a></sup>
Cali and Dougill noted that Shinto had long been associated with "an insular and protective view" of Japanese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201310_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201310-164">[164]</a></sup> They added that in the modern world, Shinto tends toward conservatism and nationalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201310_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201310-164">[164]</a></sup> In the late 1990s, Bocking noted that "an apparently regressive nationalism still seems the natural ally of some central elements" of Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix-165">[165]</a></sup> As a result of these associations, Shinto is still viewed suspiciously by various <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a> groups in Japan and by many of Japan's neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2900" data-file-width="4206" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG/220px-Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG/330px-Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG/440px-Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yasukuni_Shrine_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The actions of priests at the Yasukuni Shrine in Tokyo have generated controversy across East Asia</div></div></div>
<p>Shinto priests may face various ethical conundrums. In the 1980s, for instance, the priests at the <a href="/wiki/Suwa_Shrine_(Nagasaki)" title="Suwa Shrine (Nagasaki)">Suwa Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> debated whether to invite the crew of a U.S. Navy vessel docked at the port city to their festival celebrations given the sensitivities surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki#Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">1945 U.S. use of the atomic bomb on the city</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199666–67_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199666–67-166">[166]</a></sup> In other cases, priests have opposed construction projects on shrine-owned land, sometimes putting them at odds with other interest groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUeda1979317Rots2015221_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUeda1979317Rots2015221-167">[167]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Kaminoseki" title="Kaminoseki">Kaminoseki</a> in the early 2000s, a priest opposed the sale of shrine lands to build a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plant</a>; he was eventually pressured to resign over the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015221_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015221-168">[168]</a></sup> Another issue of considerable debate has been the activities of the <a href="/wiki/Yasukuni_Shrine" title="Yasukuni Shrine">Yasukuni Shrine</a> in Tokyo. The shrine is devoted to Japan's war dead, and in 1979 it enshrined 14 men, including <a href="/wiki/Hideki_Tojo" title="Hideki Tojo">Hideki Tojo</a>, who had been declared Class-A defendants at the 1946 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokyo_War_Crimes_Trials" title="Tokyo War Crimes Trials">Tokyo War Crimes Trials</a>. This generated both domestic and international condemnation, particularly from China and Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson200012Littleton200299Picken201118–19_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson200012Littleton200299Picken201118–19-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 21st century, Shinto has increasingly been portrayed as a nature-centred spirituality with <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalist</a> credentials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015205,_207_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015205,_207-170">[170]</a></sup> Shinto shrines have increasingly emphasised the preservation of the forests surrounding many of them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015209_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015209-171">[171]</a></sup> and several shrines have collaborated with local environmentalist campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015223_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015223-172">[172]</a></sup> In 2014, an international interreligious conference on environmental sustainability was held at the Ise shrine, attended by <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> representatives and around 700 Shinto priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015205–206_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015205–206-173">[173]</a></sup> Critical commentators have characterised the presentation of Shinto as an environmentalist movement as a rhetorical ploy rather than a concerted effort by Shinto institutions to become environmentally sustainable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015208_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015208-174">[174]</a></sup> The scholar Aike P. Rots suggested that the repositioning of Shinto as a "nature religion" may have grown in popularity as a means of disassociating the religion from controversial issues "related to war memory and imperial patronage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015210_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015210-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practice">Practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shinto tends to focus on ritual behavior rather than doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979214CaliDougill201310_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979214CaliDougill201310-175">[175]</a></sup> The philosophers James W. Boyd and Ron G. Williams stated that Shinto is "first and foremost a ritual tradition",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200533_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydWilliams200533-176">[176]</a></sup> while Picken observed that "Shinto is interested not in <i>credenda</i> but in <i>agenda</i>, not in things that should be believed but in things that should be done."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxii_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxii-177">[177]</a></sup> The scholar of religion Clark B. Offner stated that Shinto's focus was on "maintaining communal, ceremonial traditions for the purpose of human (communal) well-being".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979198_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979198-160">[160]</a></sup>
It is often difficult to distinguish Shinto practices from Japanese customs more broadly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20138_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20138-178">[178]</a></sup> with Picken observing that the "worldview of Shinto" provided the "principal source of self-understanding within the Japanese way of life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxii_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxii-177">[177]</a></sup> Nelson stated that "Shinto-based orientations and values[…] lie at the core of Japanese culture, society, and character".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19963_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19963-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shrines">Shrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Shrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="754" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg/220px-Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg/330px-Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg/440px-Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fushimi_Inari_-_Main_gate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main gate to <a href="/wiki/Fushimi_Inari-taisha" title="Fushimi Inari-taisha">Fushimi Inari-taisha</a> in Kyoto, one of the oldest shrines in Japan</div></div></div>
<p>Public spaces in which the kami are worshipped are often known under the generic term <i><a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">jinja</a></i> ("kami-place");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviiiBocking199772Earhart200436CaliDougill20137_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviiiBocking199772Earhart200436CaliDougill20137-180">[180]</a></sup> this term applies to the location rather than to a specific building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201121_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201121-181">[181]</a></sup> <i>Jinja</i> is usually translated as "shrine" in English,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436-182">[182]</a></sup> although in earlier literature was sometimes translated as "temple",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviii_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xviii-6">[6]</a></sup> a term now more commonly reserved for Japan's Buddhist structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436BreenTeeuwen20101_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436BreenTeeuwen20101-183">[183]</a></sup> There are around 100,000 public shrines in Japan;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101-184">[184]</a></sup> about 80,000 are affiliated with the Association of Shinto Shrines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxiPicken201129BreenTeeuwen20105CaliDougill20138_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxxiPicken201129BreenTeeuwen20105CaliDougill20138-185">[185]</a></sup> with another 20,000 being unaffiliated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201129_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201129-186">[186]</a></sup> They are found all over the country, from isolated rural areas to dense metropolitan ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436CaliDougill20137_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200436CaliDougill20137-187">[187]</a></sup> More specific terms are sometimes used for certain shrines depending on their function; some of the grand shrines with imperial associations are termed <i>jingū</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199771,_72_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199771,_72-188">[188]</a></sup> those devoted to the war dead are termed <i>shokonsha</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182-161">[161]</a></sup> and those linked to mountains deemed to be inhabited by kami are <i>yama-miya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997220_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997220-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jinja typically consist of complexes of multiple buildings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200268_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200268-190">[190]</a></sup> with the architectural styles of shrines having largely developed by the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199693_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199693-191">[191]</a></sup> The inner sanctuary in which the kami lives is the <i><a href="/wiki/Honden" title="Honden">honden</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199692Littleton200272Picken201143CaliDougill20137_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199692Littleton200272Picken201143CaliDougill20137-192">[192]</a></sup> Inside the honden may be stored material belonging to the kami; known as <i>shinpo</i>, this can include artworks, clothing, weapons, musical instruments, bells, and mirrors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170-193">[193]</a></sup> Typically, worshippers carry out their acts outside of the honden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137-22">[22]</a></sup> Near the honden can sometimes be found a subsidiary shrine, the <i>bekkū</i>, to another kami; the kami inhabiting this shrine is not necessarily perceived as being inferior to that in the honden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19979_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19979-194">[194]</a></sup> At some places, halls of worship have been erected, termed <i><a href="/wiki/Haiden_(Shinto)" title="Haiden (Shinto)">haiden</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199692Bocking199742Picken201143CaliDougill20137_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199692Bocking199742Picken201143CaliDougill20137-195">[195]</a></sup> On a lower level can be found the hall of offerings, known as a <i><a href="/wiki/Heiden_(Shinto)" title="Heiden (Shinto)">heiden</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199692Bocking199749Picken201143_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199692Bocking199749Picken201143-196">[196]</a></sup> Together, the building housing the honden, haiden, and heiden is called a <i>hongū</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199754_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199754-197">[197]</a></sup> In some shrines, there is a separate building in which to conduct additional ceremonies, such as weddings, known as a <i>gishikiden</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199734_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199734-198">[198]</a></sup> or a specific building in which the <i>kagura</i> dance is performed, known as the <i>kagura-den</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199782_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199782-199">[199]</a></sup> Collectively, the central buildings of a shrine are known as the <i>shaden</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997160_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997160-200">[200]</a></sup> while its precincts are known as the <i>keidaichi</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199794_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199794-201">[201]</a></sup> or <i>shin'en</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997166_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997166-202">[202]</a></sup> This precinct is surrounded by the <i>tamagaki</i> fence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997197_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997197-203">[203]</a></sup> with entry via a <i>shinmon</i> gate, which can be closed at night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997169_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997169-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg/220px-Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg/330px-Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg/440px-Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hushimi-inari-taisha_otsuka3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depictions of torii at the Fushimi Inari-taisha shrine in Kyoto</div></div></div>
<p>Shrine entrances are marked by a two-post gateway with either one or two crossbeams atop it, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Torii" title="Torii">torii</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201Bocking1997207Earhart200436CaliDougill20137_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201Bocking1997207Earhart200436CaliDougill20137-205">[205]</a></sup> The exact details of these torii varies and there are at least twenty different styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997207Picken201143_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997207Picken201143-206">[206]</a></sup> These are regarded as demarcating the area where the kami resides;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137-22">[22]</a></sup> passing under them is often viewed as a form of purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201-207">[207]</a></sup> More broadly, torii are internationally recognised symbols of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137-22">[22]</a></sup> Their architectural form is distinctly Japanese, although the decision to paint most of them in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vermillion" title="Vermillion">vermillion</a> reflects a Chinese influence dating from the <a href="/wiki/Nara_period" title="Nara period">Nara period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201120_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201120-208">[208]</a></sup> Also set at the entrances to many shrines are <i><a href="/wiki/Komainu" title="Komainu">komainu</a></i>, statues of lion or dog like animals perceived to scare off malevolent spirits;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201Bocking1997104_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201Bocking1997104-209">[209]</a></sup> typically these will come as a pair, one with its mouth open, the other with its mouth closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997104_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997104-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shrines are often set within gardens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201312_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201312-211">[211]</a></sup> or wooded groves called <i>chinju no mori</i> ("forest of the tutelary kami"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015211_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015211-212">[212]</a></sup> which vary in size from just a few trees to sizeable areas of woodland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015219_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015219-213">[213]</a></sup> Large lanterns, known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dr%C5%8D" title="Tōrō">tōrō</a></i>, are often found within these precincts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997208_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997208-214">[214]</a></sup> Shrines often have an office, known as a <i>shamusho</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199671Bocking199772_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199671Bocking199772-215">[215]</a></sup> a <i>saikan</i> where priests undergo forms of abstinence and purification prior to conducting rituals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997148_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997148-216">[216]</a></sup> and other buildings such as a priests' quarters and a storehouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201-207">[207]</a></sup> Various kiosks often sell amulets to visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199772–73_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199772–73-217">[217]</a></sup> Since the late 1940s, shrines have had to be financially self-sufficient, relying on the donations of worshippers and visitors. These funds are used to pay the wages of the priests, to finance the upkeep of the buildings, to cover the shrine's membership fees of various regional and national Shinto groups, and to contribute to disaster relief funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199677_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199677-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Shinto, it is seen as important that the places in which kami are venerated be kept clean and not neglected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201123_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201123-219">[219]</a></sup> Through to the Edo period, it was common for kami shrines to be demolished and rebuilt at a nearby location in order to remove any pollutants and ensure purity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199692_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199692-220">[220]</a></sup> This has continued into recent times at certain sites, such as the Ise Grand Shrine, which is moved to an adjacent site every two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199693Bocking1997163Nelson20004Hardacre201779–80_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199693Bocking1997163Nelson20004Hardacre201779–80-221">[221]</a></sup> Separate shrines can also be merged in a process known as <i>jinja gappei</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199773_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199773-222">[222]</a></sup> while the act of transferring the kami from one building to another is called <i>sengu</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997158_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997158-223">[223]</a></sup> Shrines may have legends about their foundation, which are known as <i>en-gi</i>. These sometimes also record miracles associated with the shrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199726_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199726-224">[224]</a></sup> From the Heian period on, the <i>en-gi</i> were often retold on picture scrolls known as <i><a href="/wiki/Emakimono" title="Emakimono">emakimono</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199726Picken201144_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199726Picken201144-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Priesthood_and_miko">Priesthood and <i>miko</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Priesthood and miko">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG/220px-Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG/330px-Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG/440px-Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miwa-shrine_Yutateshinji_A.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Yutateshinji</i></span> ceremony performed by Shinto priests at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C5%8Cmiwa_jinja" title="Ōmiwa jinja">Miwa Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sakurai,_Nara" title="Sakurai, Nara">Sakurai, Nara</a></div></div></div>
<p>Shrines may be cared for by priests, by local communities, or by families on whose property the shrine is found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20137-22">[22]</a></sup> Shinto priests are known in Japanese as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kannushi" title="Kannushi">kannushi</a></i></span>, meaning "proprietor of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199788_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199788-226">[226]</a></sup> or alternatively as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinshoku</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shinkan_(official)" title="Shinkan (official)">shinkan</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997168,_171_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997168,_171-227">[227]</a></sup> Many kannushi take on the role in a line of hereditary succession traced down specific families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUeda1979325Nelson199629_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUeda1979325Nelson199629-228">[228]</a></sup> In contemporary Japan, there are two main training universities for those wishing to become <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kannushi</i></span>, at <a href="/wiki/Kokugakuin_University" title="Kokugakuin University">Kokugakuin University</a> in Tokyo and at <a href="/wiki/Kogakkan_University" title="Kogakkan University">Kogakkan University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mie_Prefecture" title="Mie Prefecture">Mie Prefecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199629Bocking199799,_102_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199629Bocking199799,_102-229">[229]</a></sup> Priests can rise through the ranks over the course of their careers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199642_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199642-230">[230]</a></sup> The number of priests at a particular shrine can vary; some shrines can have dozens, and others have none, instead being administered by local lay volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200273Picken201131–32_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200273Picken201131–32-231">[231]</a></sup> Some priests administer to multiple small shrines, sometimes over ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201132_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201132-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>Priestly costume is largely based on the clothes worn at the imperial court during the Heian period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson200015_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson200015-233">[233]</a></sup> It includes a tall, rounded hat known as an <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">eboshi</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199725_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199725-234">[234]</a></sup> and black lacquered wooden clogs known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">asagutsu</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19977Picken201144_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19977Picken201144-235">[235]</a></sup> The outer garment worn by a priest, usually colored black, red, or light blue, is the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hō</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199753_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199753-236">[236]</a></sup> or the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ikan</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199758_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199758-142">[142]</a></sup> A white silk version of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ikan</i></span>, used for formal occasions, is known as the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">saifuku</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199758,_146_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199758,_146-237">[237]</a></sup> Another priestly robe is the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kariginu</i></span>, which is modelled on Heian-style hunting garments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199789–90_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199789–90-238">[238]</a></sup> Also part of standard priestly attire is a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hiōgi</i></span> fan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199751_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199751-239">[239]</a></sup> while during rituals, priests carry a flat piece of wood known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaku_(ritual_baton)" title="Shaku (ritual baton)">shaku</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997162_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997162-240">[240]</a></sup> This costume is generally more ornate than the sombre garments worn by Japanese Buddhist monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson200015_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson200015-233">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg/220px-Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg/330px-Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg/440px-Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kamogawa_ceremony_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Miko performing a Shinto ceremony near the <a href="/wiki/Kamo_River" title="Kamo River">Kamo River</a></div></div></div>
<p>The chief priest at a shrine is the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">gūji</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212Nelson1996186Bocking199739BoydWilliams200533_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212Nelson1996186Bocking199739BoydWilliams200533-241">[241]</a></sup> Larger shrines may also have an assistant head priest, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">gon-gūji</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212-242">[242]</a></sup> As with teachers, instructors, and Buddhist clergy, Shinto priests are often referred to as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Sensei" title="Sensei">sensei</a></i></span> by lay practitioners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996179_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996179-243">[243]</a></sup> Historically, there were female priests although they were largely pushed out of their positions in 1868.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996123_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996123-244">[244]</a></sup> During the Second World War, women were again allowed to become priests to fill the void caused by large numbers of men being enlisted in the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996124_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996124-245">[245]</a></sup> By the late 1990s, around 90% of priests were male, 10% female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200298_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200298-116">[116]</a></sup> Priests are free to marry and have children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996124_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996124-245">[245]</a></sup>  At smaller shrines, priests often have other full-time jobs, and serve only as priests during special occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979212-242">[242]</a></sup>
Before certain major festivals, priests may undergo a period of abstinence from sexual relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199643_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199643-246">[246]</a></sup> Some of those involved in festivals also abstain from a range of other things, such as consuming tea, coffee, or alcohol, immediately prior to the events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996141_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996141-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>The priests are assisted by <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">jinja miko</i></span>, sometimes referred to as "shrine-maidens" in English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997121_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997121-248">[248]</a></sup> These <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Miko" title="Miko">miko</a></i></span> are typically unmarried,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199647Bocking1997121_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199647Bocking1997121-249">[249]</a></sup> although not necessarily virgins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199647_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199647-250">[250]</a></sup> In many cases they are the daughters of a priest or a practitioner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997121_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997121-248">[248]</a></sup> They are subordinate to the priests in the shrine hierarchy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996124–125_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996124–125-251">[251]</a></sup> Their most important role is in the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kagura" title="Kagura">kagura</a></i></span> dance, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">otome-mai</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996125_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996125-252">[252]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Miko</i></span> receive only a small salary but gain respect from members of the local community and learn skills such as cooking, calligraphy, painting, and etiquette which can benefit them when later searching for employment or a marriage partner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996125_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996125-252">[252]</a></sup> They generally do not live at the shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996125_252-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996125-252">[252]</a></sup> Sometimes they fill other roles, such as being secretaries in the shrine offices or clerks at the information desks, or as waitresses at the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">naorai</i></span> feasts. They also assist <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kannushi</i></span> in ceremonial rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996125_252-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996125-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Visits_to_shrines">Visits to shrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Visits to shrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg/220px-%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg/330px-%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg/440px-%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%AD%82%E5%85%A5%E3%82%8C%E3%81%AB%E4%BC%B4%E3%81%86%E5%B1%B1%E8%BB%8A%EF%BC%88%E8%81%96%E6%AD%A6%E5%B1%B1%EF%BC%89%E3%81%AE%E4%BF%AE%E7%A5%93%E3%80%81%E5%A7%A5%E7%A5%9E%E5%A4%A7%E7%A5%9E%E5%AE%AE%E3%81%AE%E9%B3%A5%E5%B1%85%E5%89%8D%E3%81%AB%E3%81%A6%EF%BC%882018%E5%B9%B48%E6%9C%889%E6%97%A5%E6%92%AE%E5%BD%B1%EF%BC%89.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A priest purifies the area in front of the residence of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.</div></div></div>
<p>Visits to the shrine are termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sankei</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997152_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997152-253">[253]</a></sup> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">jinja mairi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> Some individuals visit the shrines daily, often on their morning route to work;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> they typically take only a few minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> Usually, a worshipper will approach the honden, placing a monetary offering in a box and then ringing a bell to call the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>'s attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201–202Littleton200272CaliDougill201311_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201–202Littleton200272CaliDougill201311-255">[255]</a></sup> Then, they bow, clap, and stand while silently offering a prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979204BreenTeeuwen20103CaliDougill201311_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979204BreenTeeuwen20103CaliDougill201311-256">[256]</a></sup> The clapping is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kashiwade</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hakushu</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199743,_90_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199743,_90-257">[257]</a></sup> the prayers or supplications as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kigan</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199796_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199796-258">[258]</a></sup> This individual worship is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hairei</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199742_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199742-259">[259]</a></sup> More broadly, ritual prayers to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Norito" title="Norito">norito</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135-260">[260]</a></sup> while the coins offered are <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">saisen</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997149_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997149-261">[261]</a></sup> At the shrine, individuals offering prayers are not necessarily praying to a specific <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> A worshipper may not know the name of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> residing at the shrine nor how many <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are believed to dwell there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202CaliDougill201311_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979202CaliDougill201311-262">[262]</a></sup> Unlike in certain other religions, Shinto shrines do not have weekly services that practitioners are expected to attend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412-263">[263]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Shinto practitioners do not offer their prayers to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> directly, but rather request that a priest offer them on their behalf; these prayers are known as <i>kitō</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199798_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199798-264">[264]</a></sup> Many individuals approach the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> asking for pragmatic requests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996116_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996116-265">[265]</a></sup> Requests for rain, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">amagoi</i></span> ("rain-soliciting") have been found across Japan, with Inari a popular choice for such requests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19973Picken201136_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19973Picken201136-266">[266]</a></sup>
Other prayers reflect more contemporary concerns. For instance, people may ask that the priest approaches the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> so as to purify their car in the hope that this will prevent it from being involved in an accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996116Bocking1997114_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996116Bocking1997114-267">[267]</a></sup> Similarly, transport companies often request purification rites for new buses or airplanes which are about to go into service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997108_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997108-268">[268]</a></sup> Before a building is constructed, it is common for either private individuals or the construction company to employ a Shinto priest to come to the land being developed and perform the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">jichinsai</i></span>, or earth sanctification ritual. This purifies the site and asks the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to bless it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996190–196Bocking199768_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996190–196Bocking199768-269">[269]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg/220px-Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg/330px-Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg/440px-Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinto_Priest_Blessing_a_Car_at_the_Hokkaido_Shrine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Previa" title="Toyota Previa">Toyota Estima</a> being blessed at the <a href="/wiki/Hokkaid%C5%8D_Shrine" title="Hokkaidō Shrine">Hokkaidō Shrine</a></div></div></div>
<p>People often ask the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to help offset inauspicious events that may affect them. For instance, in Japanese culture, the age 33 is seen as being unlucky for women and the age 42 for men, and thus people can ask the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to offset any ill-fortune associated with being this age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996183_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996183-270">[270]</a></sup> Certain directions can also be seen as being inauspicious for certain people at certain times and thus people can approach the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> asking them to offset this problem if they have to travel in one of these unlucky directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996183_270-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996183-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimage</a> has long been important in Japanese religion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xvii–xviii_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987xvii–xviii-271">[271]</a></sup> with pilgrimages to Shinto shrines called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Junrei" title="Junrei">junrei</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199780_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199780-272">[272]</a></sup> A round of pilgrimages, whereby individuals visit a series of shrines and other sacred sites that are part of an established circuit, is known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">junpai</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199780_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199780-272">[272]</a></sup> An individual leading these pilgrims, is sometimes termed a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sendatsu</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997158_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997158-223">[223]</a></sup> For many centuries, people have also visited the shrines for primarily cultural and recreational reasons, as opposed to spiritual ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> Many of the shrines are recognised as sites of historical importance and some are classified as <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites" title="World Heritage Sites">World Heritage Sites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311_254-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201311-254">[254]</a></sup> Shrines such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shimogamo_Jinja" title="Shimogamo Jinja">Shimogamo Jinja</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fushimi_Inari_Taisha" title="Fushimi Inari Taisha">Fushimi Inari Taisha</a> in Kyoto, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_Jing%C5%AB" title="Meiji Jingū">Meiji Jingū</a> in Tokyo, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atsuta_Jing%C5%AB" title="Atsuta Jingū">Atsuta Jingū</a> in Nagoya are among Japan's most popular tourist sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERots2015221_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERots2015221-168">[168]</a></sup> Many shrines have a unique rubber-stamp seal which visitors can get printed into their <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sutanpu bukku</i></span> or stamp book, demonstrating the different shrines they have visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997192_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997192-273">[273]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Harae_and_h.C5.8Dbei"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Harae_and_hōbei"><i>Harae</i> and <i>hōbei</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Harae and hōbei">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Harae" title="Harae">Harae</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg/220px-ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg/330px-ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg/440px-ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ItsukushimaBasin7406.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shinto rituals begin with a process of purification, often involving the washing of the hands and mouth at the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">temizu</i></span> basin; this example is at Itsukushima Jinja.</div></div></div>
<p>Shinto rituals begin with a process of purification, or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">harae</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199639Bocking199745_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199639Bocking199745-274">[274]</a></sup> Using fresh water or salt water, this is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">misogi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997124-143">[143]</a></sup> At shrines, this entails sprinkling this water onto the face and hands, a procedure known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">temizu</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199745_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199745-275">[275]</a></sup> using a font known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temizuya" title="Temizuya">temizuya</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199691_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199691-276">[276]</a></sup> Another form of purification at the start of a Shinto rite entails waving a white paper streamer or wand known as the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haraigushi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199639,_46Bocking199745_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199639,_46Bocking199745-277">[277]</a></sup> When not in use, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haraigushi</i></span> is usually kept in a stand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199745_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199745-275">[275]</a></sup> The priest waves the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haraigushi</i></span> horizontally over a person or object being purified in a movement known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sa-yu-sa</i></span> ("left-right-left").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199745_275-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199745-275">[275]</a></sup> Sometimes, instead of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haraigushi</i></span>, the purification is carried out with an <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">o-nusa</i></span>, a branch of evergreen to which strips of paper have been attached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199745_275-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199745-275">[275]</a></sup> The waving of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haraigushi</i></span> is often followed by an additional act of purification, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shubatsu</i></span>, in which the priest sprinkles water, salt, or brine over those assembled from a wooden box called the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">'en-to-oke</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">magemono</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997184_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997184-278">[278]</a></sup>
</p><p>The acts of purification accomplished, petitions known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">norito</i></span> are spoken to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640-279">[279]</a></sup> This is followed by an appearance by the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">miko</i></span>, who commence in a slow circular motion before the main altar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640_279-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640-279">[279]</a></sup> Offerings are then presented to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> by being placed on a table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640_279-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640-279">[279]</a></sup> This act is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hōbei</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199753_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199753-236">[236]</a></sup> the offerings themselves as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">saimotsu</i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997148_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997148-216">[216]</a></sup> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sonae-mono</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187-280">[280]</a></sup> Historically, the offerings given the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> included food, cloth, swords, and horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313–14_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201313–14-281">[281]</a></sup> In the contemporary period, lay worshippers usually give gifts of money to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> while priests generally offer them food, drink, and sprigs of the sacred <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakaki" title="Sakaki">sakaki</a></i></span> tree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201314-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">Animal sacrifices</a> are not considered appropriate offerings, as the shedding of blood is seen as a polluting act that necessitates purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199664_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199664-282">[282]</a></sup> The offerings presented are sometimes simple and sometimes more elaborate; at the Grand Shrine of Ise, for instance, 100 styles of food are laid out as offerings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640_279-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640-279">[279]</a></sup> The choice of offerings will often be tailored to the specific <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and occasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Offerings of food and drink are specifically termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinsen</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997170-193">[193]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">Sake</a>, or rice wine, is a very common offering to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997150_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997150-283">[283]</a></sup> After the offerings have been given, people often sip rice wine known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">o-miki</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640_279-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640-279">[279]</a></sup> Drinking the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">o-miki</i></span> wine is seen as a form of communion with the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199653_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199653-284">[284]</a></sup> On important occasions, a feast is then held, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">naorai</i></span>, inside a banquet hall attached to the shrine complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199640,_53_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199640,_53-285">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are believed to enjoy music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199649_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199649-286">[286]</a></sup> One style of music performed at shrines is <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">gagaku</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199649Bocking199733_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199649Bocking199733-287">[287]</a></sup> Instruments used include three reeds (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">fue</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sho</i></span>, and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hichiriki</i></span>), the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">yamato-koto</i></span>, and the "three drums" (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">taiko</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kakko</i></span>, and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shōko</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199733_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199733-288">[288]</a></sup> Other musical styles performed at shrines can have a more limited focus. At shrines such as <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Charano_Shrine" title="Ōharano Shrine">Ōharano Shrine</a> in Kyoto, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">azuma-asobi</i></span> ("eastern entertainment") music is performed on April 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19978_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19978-98">[98]</a></sup> Also in Kyoto, various festivals make use of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Dengaku" title="Dengaku">dengaku</a></i></span> style of music and dance, which originated from rice-planting songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199722_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199722-289">[289]</a></sup> During rituals, people visiting the shrine are expected to sit in the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Seiza" title="Seiza">seiza</a></i></span> style, with their legs tucked beneath their bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996214_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996214-290">[290]</a></sup> To avoid cramps, individuals who hold this position for a lengthy period of time may periodically move their legs and flex their heels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996214–215_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996214–215-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_shrines">Home shrines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Home shrines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kamidana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="249" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kamidana.jpg/300px-Kamidana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Kamidana.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kamidana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kamidana</i></span> displaying a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shimenawa" title="Shimenawa">shimenawa</a></i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shide_(Shinto)" title="Shide (Shinto)">shide</a></i></span></div></div></div>
<p>Having seen their popularity increase in the Meiji era,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785-292">[292]</a></sup> many Shinto practitioners also have a family shrine, or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kamidana" title="Kamidana">kamidana</a></i></span> ("kami shelf"), in their home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979200Nelson1996184Littleton200273Earhart200411_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979200Nelson1996184Littleton200273Earhart200411-293">[293]</a></sup> These usually consist of shelves placed at an elevated position in the living room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979200–201_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979200–201-294">[294]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kamidana</i></span> can also be found in workplaces, restaurants, shops, and ocean-going ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785Earhart200411_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785Earhart200411-295">[295]</a></sup> Some public shrines sell entire <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kamidana</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201131_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201131-296">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kamidana</i></span>, many Japanese households also have <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Butsudan" title="Butsudan">butsudan</a></i></span>, Buddhist altars enshrining the ancestors of the family;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Earhart200411_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199713Earhart200411-297">[297]</a></sup> ancestral reverence remains an important aspect of Japanese religious tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201139_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201139-131">[131]</a></sup> In the rare instances where Japanese individuals are given a Shinto funeral rather than a Buddhist one, a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">tama-ya</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mitama-ya</i></span>, or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sorei-sha</i></span> shrine may be erected in the home in place of a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">butsudan</i></span>. This will be typically placed below the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kamidana</i></span> and include symbols of the resident ancestral spirit, for instance a mirror or a scroll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997198_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997198-298">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kamidana</i></span> often enshrine the kami of a nearby public shrine as well as a tutelary kami associated with the house's occupants or their profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785_292-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785-292">[292]</a></sup> They can be decorated with miniature torii and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shimenawa</i></span> and include amulets obtained from public shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785_292-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785-292">[292]</a></sup> They often contain a stand on which to place offerings;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201_207-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979201-207">[207]</a></sup> daily offerings of rice, salt, and water are placed there, with sake and other items also offered on special days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785Littleton200274_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785Littleton200274-299">[299]</a></sup> These domestic rituals often take place early in the morning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200281_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200281-300">[300]</a></sup> and prior to conducting them, practitioners often bathe, rinse their mouth, or wash their hands as a form of purification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979203_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979203-301">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>Household Shinto can focus attention on the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">dōzoku-shin</i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> who are perceived to be ancestral to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">dōzoku</i></span> or extended kinship group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199724Picken201175–76_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199724Picken201175–76-302">[302]</a></sup> A small shrine for the ancestors of a household are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">soreisha</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187_280-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187-280">[280]</a></sup> Small village shrines containing the tutelary kami of an extended family are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">iwai-den</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199766_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199766-303">[303]</a></sup> In addition to the temple shrines and the household shrines, Shinto also features small wayside shrines known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hokora" title="Hokora">hokora</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199754_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199754-197">[197]</a></sup> Other open spaces used for the worship of kami are <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iwasaka" title="Iwasaka">iwasaka</a></i></span>, an area surrounded by sacred rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199765_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199765-304">[304]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ema.2C_divination.2C_and_amulets"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ema,_divination,_and_amulets">Ema, divination, and amulets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ema, divination, and amulets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg/220px-Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg/330px-Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg/440px-Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ema_of_Izumo_taissha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A selection of wooden <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ema</i></span> hanging up at a Shinto shrine</div></div></div>
<p>A common feature of Shinto shrines is the provision of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ema_(Shinto)" title="Ema (Shinto)">ema</a></i></span>, small wooden plaques onto which practitioners will write a wish or desire that they would like to see fulfilled. The practitioner's message is written on one side of the plaque, while on the other is usually a printed picture or pattern related to the shrine itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199725–26_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199725–26-305">[305]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ema</i></span> are provided both at Shinto shrines and Buddhist temples in Japan;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199725_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199725-234">[234]</a></sup> unlike most amulets, which are taken away from the shrine, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ema</i></span> are typically left there as a message for the resident <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199726_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199726-224">[224]</a></sup> Those administering the shrine will then often burn all of the collected <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ema</i></span> at new year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199726_224-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199726-224">[224]</a></sup>
