</p><p>Divination is the focus of many Shinto rituals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201318_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201318-306">[306]</a></sup> with various forms of divination used by its practitioners, some introduced from China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201173_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201173-307">[307]</a></sup> Among the ancient forms of divination found in Japan are <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">rokuboku</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kiboku</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201317_308-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill201317-308">[308]</a></sup> Several forms of divination entailing <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archery</a> are also practiced in Shintō, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Yabusame" title="Yabusame">yabusame</a></i></span>, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">omato-shinji</i></span>, and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mato-i</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken2011_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken2011-309">[309]</a></sup> Kitagawa stated that there could be "no doubt" that various types of "shamanic diviners" played a role in early Japanese religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198739_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198739-310">[310]</a></sup> A form of divination previously common in Japan was <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bokusen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bokusen (page does not exist)">bokusen</a></i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">uranai</i></span>, which often used tortoise shells; it is still used in some places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201150_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201150-311">[311]</a></sup>
</p><p>A form of divination that is popular at Shinto shrines are the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omikuji" title="Omikuji">omikuji</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997138Picken201174_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997138Picken201174-312">[312]</a></sup> These are small slips of paper which are obtained from the shrine (for a donation) and which are then read to reveal a prediction for the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997137–138_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997137–138-313">[313]</a></sup> Those who receive a bad prediction often then tie the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">omikuji</i></span> to a nearby tree or frame set up for the purpose. This act is seen as rejecting the prediction, a process called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sute-mikuji</i></span>, and thus avoiding the misfortune it predicted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997139Picken201174_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997139Picken201174-314">[314]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1664" data-file-width="2496" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg/220px-Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg/330px-Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg/440px-Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hiromine-jinja_by_CR_38.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A frame at a shrine where omikuji are tied</div></div></div>
<p>The use of <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a> are widely sanctioned and popular in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412-263">[263]</a></sup> These may be made of paper, wood, cloth, metal, or plastic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412_263-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200412-263">[263]</a></sup>
<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ofuda" title="Ofuda">Ofuda</a></i></span> act as amulets to keep off misfortune and also serve as talismans to bring benefits and good luck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135-260">[260]</a></sup> They typically comprise a tapering piece of wood onto which the name of the shrine and its enshrined <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> are written or printed. The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ofuda</i></span> is then wrapped inside white paper and tied up with a colored thread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135–136_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135–136-315">[315]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Ofuda</i></span> are provided both at Shinto shrines and Buddhist temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135_260-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997135-260">[260]</a></sup> Another type of amulet provided at shrines and temples are the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Omamori" title="Omamori">omamori</a></i></span>, which are traditionally small, brightly colored drawstring bags with the name of the shrine written on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997138_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997138-316">[316]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Omamori</i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ofuda</i></span> are sometimes placed within a charm bag known as a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kinchaku" title="Kinchaku">kinchaku</a></i></span>, typically worn by small children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199796_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199796-258">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>At new year, many shrines sell <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hama_Yumi" title="Hama Yumi">hamaya</a></i></span> (an "evil-destroying arrows"), which people can purchase and keep in their home over the coming year to bring good luck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199743–44_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199743–44-317">[317]</a></sup>
A <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Daruma_doll" title="Daruma doll">daruma</a></i></span> is a round, paper doll of the Indian monk, <a href="/wiki/Bodhidharma" title="Bodhidharma">Bodhidharma</a>. The recipient makes a wish and paints one eye; when the goal is accomplished, the recipient paints the other eye. While this is a Buddhist practice, darumas can be found at shrines, as well. These dolls are very common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-318">[318]</a></sup>
Other protective items include <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">dorei</i></span>, which are earthenware bells that are used to pray for good fortune. These bells are usually in the shapes of the zodiacal animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_318-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-318">[318]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Inuhariko</i></span> are paper dogs that are used to induce and to bless good births.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_318-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-318">[318]</a></sup> Collectively, these talismans through which home to manipulate events and influence spirits, as well as related mantras and rites for the same purpose, are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">majinai</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997114–15_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997114–15-319">[319]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kagura"><i>Kagura</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Kagura">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG/220px-Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG/330px-Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG/440px-Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ymananashi-oka_shrine_Daidai_Kagura_A.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kagura</i></span> traditional dance performed at the Yamanashi-oka shrine</div></div></div>
<p><span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kagura" title="Kagura">Kagura</a></i></span> describes the music and dance performed for the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Bocking199781_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Bocking199781-320">[320]</a></sup> the term may have originally derived from  <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami no kura</i></span> ("seat of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKobayashi19813_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKobayashi19813-321">[321]</a></sup> Throughout Japanese history, dance has played an important culture role and in Shinto it is regarded as having the capacity to pacify <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198723_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198723-322">[322]</a></sup> There is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythological</a> tale of how <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kagura</i></span> dance came into existence. According to the <i>Kojiki</i> and the <i>Nihon Shoki</i>, <a href="/wiki/Ame-no-Uzume" title="Ame-no-Uzume">Ame-no-Uzume</a> performed a dance to entice Amaterasu out of the cave in which she had hidden herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198723Bocking199781Picken201168_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198723Bocking199781Picken201168-323">[323]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two broad types of kagura.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199781_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199781-324">[324]</a></sup> One is Imperial kagura, also known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mikagura</i></span>. This style was developed in the imperial court and is still performed on <a href="/wiki/Three_Palace_Sanctuaries" title="Three Palace Sanctuaries">imperial grounds</a> every December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199781–82_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199781–82-325">[325]</a></sup> It is also performed at the Imperial harvest festival and at major shrines such as Ise, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamo_Shrines" title="Kamo Shrines">Kamo</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iwashimizu_Shrine" title="Iwashimizu Shrine">Iwashimizu Hachiman-gū</a>. It is performed by singers and musicians using <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shakubyoshi" title="Shakubyoshi">shakubyoshi</a></i></span> wooden clappers, a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hichiriki" title="Hichiriki">hichiriki</a></i></span>, a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kagura-bue</i></span> flute, and a six-stringed zither.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199782_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199782-199">[199]</a></sup> The other main type is <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">sato-kagura</i></span>, descended from <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mikagura</i></span> and performed at shrines across Japan. Depending on the style, it is performed by <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">miko</i></span> or by actors wearing masks to portray various mythological figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199782,_155_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199782,_155-326">[326]</a></sup> These actors are accompanied by a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hayashi</i></span> band using flutes and drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199782_199-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199782-199">[199]</a></sup> There are also other, regional types of kagura.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199782_199-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199782-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>Music plays a very important role in the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kagura</i></span> performance. Everything from the setup of the instruments to the most subtle sounds and the arrangement of the music is crucial to encouraging the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to come down and dance. The songs are used as magical devices to summon the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and as prayers for blessings. Rhythm patterns of five and seven are common, possibly relating to the Shinto belief of the twelve generations of heavenly and earthly deities. There is also vocal accompaniment called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami uta</i></span> in which the drummer sings sacred songs to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>. Often the vocal accompaniment is overshadowed by the drumming and instruments, reinforcing that the vocal aspect of the music is more for <a href="/wiki/Incantation" title="Incantation">incantation</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aoi_Matsuri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="5078" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aoi_Matsuri.jpg/220px-Aoi_Matsuri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aoi_Matsuri.jpg/330px-Aoi_Matsuri.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Aoi_Matsuri.jpg/440px-Aoi_Matsuri.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aoi_Matsuri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Participants in a procession for Aoi Matsuri in Kyoto</div></div></div>
<p>Public festivals are commonly termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matsuri" title="Matsuri">matsuri</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200281BoydWilliams200536Picken20119_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200281BoydWilliams200536Picken20119-328">[328]</a></sup> although this term has varied meanings—"festival," "worship," "celebration," "rite," or "prayer"—and no direct translation into English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997117_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997117-329">[329]</a></sup> Picken suggested that the festival was "the central act of Shinto worship" because Shinto was a "community- and family-based" religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxvi_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken1994xxvi-330">[330]</a></sup> Most mark the seasons of the agricultural year and involve offerings being directed to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> in thanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997117–118_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997117–118-331">[331]</a></sup> According to a traditional <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a>, Shinto shrines should hold their festival celebrations on <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hare-no-hi</i></span> or "clear" days", the days of the new, full, and half moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199746_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199746-332">[332]</a></sup> Other days, known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ke-no-hi</i></span>, were generally avoided for festivities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199746_332-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199746-332">[332]</a></sup> However, since the late 20th century, many shrines have held their festival celebrations on the Saturday or Sunday closest to the date so that fewer individuals will be working and will be able to attend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996224Earhart2004222_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996224Earhart2004222-333">[333]</a></sup> Each town or village often has its own festival, centred on a local shrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200281_300-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200281-300">[300]</a></sup> For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Aoi_Matsuri" title="Aoi Matsuri">Aoi Matsuri</a> festival, held on 15 May to pray for an abundant grain harvest, takes place at shrines in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19976Picken201142_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19976Picken201142-334">[334]</a></sup> while the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chichibu_Yo-Matsuri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chichibu Yo-Matsuri (page does not exist)">Chichibu Yo-Matsuri</a> takes place on 2–3 December in <a href="/wiki/Chichibu,_Saitama" title="Chichibu, Saitama">Chichibu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201159_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201159-335">[335]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spring festivals are called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">haru-matsuri</i></span> and often incorporate prayers for a good harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199746_332-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199746-332">[332]</a></sup> They sometimes involve <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ta-asobi</i></span> ceremonies, in which rice is ritually planted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199746_332-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199746-332">[332]</a></sup> Summer festivals are termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">natsu-matsuri</i></span> and are usually focused on protecting the crops against pests and other threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997132_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997132-336">[336]</a></sup> Autumn festivals are known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">aki-matsuri</i></span> and primarily focus on thanking the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> for the rice or other harvest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking19972Picken201135_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking19972Picken201135-337">[337]</a></sup> The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niiname-sai" title="Niiname-sai">Niiname-sai</a></i></span>, or festival of new rice, is held across many Shinto shrines on 23 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996170_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996170-338">[338]</a></sup> The emperor also conducts a ceremony to mark this festival, at which he presents the first fruits of the harvest to the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> at midnight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205-339">[339]</a></sup> Winter festivals, called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">fuyu no matsuri</i></span> often feature on welcoming in the spring, expelling evil, and calling in good influences for the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199732_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199732-340">[340]</a></sup> There is little difference between winter festivals and specific new year festivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199732_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199732-340">[340]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg/220px-Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg/330px-Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg/440px-Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomioka_hachimangu10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Procession of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Fukagawa_Matsuri" title="Fukagawa Matsuri">Fukagawa Matsuri</a> festival in Tokyo</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">season of the new year</a> is called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shogatsu</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182Littleton200280_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182Littleton200280-341">[341]</a></sup> On the last day of the year (31 December), <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">omisoka</i></span>, practitioners usually clean their household shrines in preparation for New Year's Day (1 January), <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ganjitsu</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997139_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997139-342">[342]</a></sup> Many people visit public shrines to celebrate new year;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Nelson1996199Littleton200280BreenTeeuwen20103_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Nelson1996199Littleton200280BreenTeeuwen20103-343">[343]</a></sup> this "first visit" of the year is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hatsumōde</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hatsumairi</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199747BreenTeeuwen20103_344-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199747BreenTeeuwen20103-344">[344]</a></sup> There, they buy amulets and talismans to bring them good fortune over the coming year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996208_345-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996208-345">[345]</a></sup> To celebrate this festival, many Japanese put up rope known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shimenawa" title="Shimenawa">shimenawa</a></i></span> on their homes and places of business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996206Bocking1997163_346-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996206Bocking1997163-346">[346]</a></sup> Some also put up <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kadomatsu" title="Kadomatsu">kadomatsu</a></i></span> ("gateway pine"), an arrangement of pine branches, plum tree, and bamboo sticks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996206Bocking199781_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996206Bocking199781-347">[347]</a></sup> Also displayed are <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kazari</i></span>, which are smaller and more colourful; their purpose is to keep away misfortune and attract good fortune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199793_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199793-136">[136]</a></sup> In many places, new year celebrations incorporate <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadaka_matsuri" title="Hadaka matsuri">hadaka matsuri</a></i></span> ("naked festivals") in which men dressed only in a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Fundoshi" title="Fundoshi">fundoshi</a></i></span> loincloth engage in a particular activity, such as fighting over a specific object or immersing themselves in a river.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199741_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199741-348">[348]</a></sup>
</p><p>A common feature of festivals are processions or parades known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">gyōretsu</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199739–40_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199739–40-349">[349]</a></sup> These can be raucous, with many participants being drunk;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Nelson1996133_350-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979205Nelson1996133-350">[350]</a></sup> Breen and Teeuwen characterised them as having a "carnivalesque atmosphere".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20104_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20104-351">[351]</a></sup> They are often understood as having a regenerative effect on both the participants and the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996134_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996134-352">[352]</a></sup> During these processions, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> travel in portable shrines known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Mikoshi" title="Mikoshi">mikoshi</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996140Bocking1997122Littleton200282BreenTeeuwen20104_353-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996140Bocking1997122Littleton200282BreenTeeuwen20104-353">[353]</a></sup> In various cases the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">mikoshi</i></span> undergo <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hamaori</i></span> ("going down to the beach"), a process by which they are carried to the sea shore and sometimes into the sea, either by bearers or a boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199743_354-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199743-354">[354]</a></sup> For instance, in the Okunchi festival held in the southwestern city of <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> of the <a href="/wiki/Suwa_Shrine_(Nagasaki)" title="Suwa Shrine (Nagasaki)">Suwa Shrine</a> are paraded down to Ohato, where they are placed in a shrine there for several days before being paraded back to Suwa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996152–154_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996152–154-355">[355]</a></sup> These sort of celebrations are often organized largely by members of the local community rather than by the priests themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20104_351-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20104-351">[351]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rites_of_passage">Rites of passage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rites of passage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The formal recognition of events is given great importance in Japanese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson199634_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson199634-356">[356]</a></sup> A common ritual, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">hatsumiyamairi</i></span>, entails a child's first visit to a Shinto shrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996161Bocking199747BreenTeeuwen20103_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996161Bocking199747BreenTeeuwen20103-357">[357]</a></sup> A tradition holds that, if a boy he should be brought to the shrine on the thirty-second day after birth, and if a girl she should be brought on the thirty-third day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199747_358-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199747-358">[358]</a></sup> Historically, the child was commonly brought to the shrine not by the mother, who was considered impure after birth, but by another female relative; since the late 20th century it has been more common for the mother to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199747_358-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199747-358">[358]</a></sup> Another rite of passage, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">saiten-sai</i></span> or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">seijin shiki</i></span>, is a coming of age ritual marking the transition to adulthood and occurs when an individual is around twenty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996212–213Bocking1997156_359-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996212–213Bocking1997156-359">[359]</a></sup> Wedding ceremonies are often carried out at Shinto shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200415_360-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200415-360">[360]</a></sup> These are called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shinzen kekkon</i></span> ("a wedding before the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>") and were popularised in the Meiji period; prior to this, weddings were commonly performed in the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997178–179_361-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997178–179-361">[361]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Japan, funerals tend to take place at Buddhist temples and involve cremation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200292Earhart200415_362-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200292Earhart200415-362">[362]</a></sup> with Shinto funerals being rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201139_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201139-131">[131]</a></sup> Bocking noted that most Japanese people are "still 'born Shinto' yet 'die Buddhist'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997ix-165">[165]</a></sup> In Shinto thought, contact with death is seen as imparting impurity (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kegare</i></span>); the period following this contact is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kibuku</i></span> and is associated with various taboos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199795_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199795-363">[363]</a></sup> In cases when dead humans are enshrined as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, the physical remains of the dead are not stored at the shrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201119_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201119-364">[364]</a></sup> Although not common, there have been examples of funerals conducted through Shinto rites. The earliest examples are known from the mid-17th century; these occurred in certain areas of Japan and had the support of the local authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenney2000241_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenney2000241-365">[365]</a></sup>
Following the Meiji Restoration, in 1868 the government recognised specifically Shinto funerals for Shinto priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187Kenney2000240_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997187Kenney2000240-366">[366]</a></sup> Five years later, this was extended to cover the entire Japanese population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenney2000240–241_367-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenney2000240–241-367">[367]</a></sup> Despite this Meiji promotion of Shinto funerals, the majority of the population continued to have Buddhist funeral rites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKenney2000241_365-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKenney2000241-365">[365]</a></sup> In recent decades, Shinto funerals have usually been reserved for Shinto priests and for members of certain Shinto sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997188_368-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997188-368">[368]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremation</a>, the normal funerary process in Japan, the ashes of a priest may be interred near to the shrine, but not inside its precincts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201171_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201171-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ancestral reverence remains an important part of Japanese religious custom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201139_131-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201139-131">[131]</a></sup> The invocation of the dead, and especially the war dead, is known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shо̄kon</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997182-161">[161]</a></sup> Various rites reference this. For instance, at the largely Buddhist festival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bon_Festival" title="Bon Festival">Bon</a>, the souls of the ancestors are believed to visit the living, and are then sent away in a ritual called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shо̄rо̄ nagashi</i></span>, by which lanterns are inserted into small boats, often made of paper, and placed in a river to float downstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997183_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997183-369">[369]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirit_mediumship_and_healing">Spirit mediumship and healing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Spirit mediumship and healing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inako_2006-10-09.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Inako_2006-10-09.jpg/220px-Inako_2006-10-09.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Inako_2006-10-09.jpg/330px-Inako_2006-10-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Inako_2006-10-09.jpg/440px-Inako_2006-10-09.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inako_2006-10-09.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">itako</i></span> at the autumn Inako Taisai festival at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Osore" title="Mount Osore">Mount Osore</a>, Aomori Prefecture, Japan</div></div></div>
<p>Shinto practitioners believe that the <i>kami</i> can possess a human being and then speak through them, a process known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami-gakari</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199785–86_370-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199785–86-370">[370]</a></sup> Several new religious movements drawing upon Shinto, such as <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oomoto" title="Oomoto">Oomoto</a>, were founded by individuals claiming to be guided by a possessing kami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199786_371-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199786-371">[371]</a></sup> The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">takusen</i></span> is an <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracle</a> that is passed from the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> via the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997197_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997197-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Itako" title="Itako">itako</a></i></span> and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ichiko</i></span> are blind women who train to become <a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">spiritual mediums</a>, traditionally in Japan's northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tohoku" title="Tohoku">Tohoku</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199763_372-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199763-372">[372]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Itako</i></span> train under other <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">itako</i></span> from childhood, memorialising sacred texts and prayers, fasting, and undertaking acts of severe asceticism, through which they are believed to cultivate supernatural powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199763_372-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199763-372">[372]</a></sup> In an initiation ceremony, a kami is believed to possess the young woman, and the two are then ritually "married". After this, the kami becomes her tutelary spirit and she will henceforth be able to call upon it, and a range of other spirits, in the future. Through contacting these spirits, she is able to convey their messages to the living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199763_372-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199763-372">[372]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Itako</i></span> usually carry out their rituals independent of the shrine system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199763–64_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199763–64-373">[373]</a></sup> Japanese culture also includes spiritual healers known as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ogamiya-san</i></span> whose work involves invoking both kami and Buddhas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997136_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997136-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shinto" title="History of Shinto">History of Shinto</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_development">Early development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Early development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2863" data-file-width="2147" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg/220px-DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg/330px-DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg/440px-DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DotakuBronzeBellLateYayoi3rdCenturyCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Yayoi period <i>dotaku</i> bell; these probably played a key role in kami rites at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719-73">[73]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Earhart commented that Shinto ultimately "emerged from the beliefs and practices of prehistoric Japan",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20042_374-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20042-374">[374]</a></sup> although Kitagawa noted that it was questionable whether prehistoric Japanese religions could be accurately termed "early Shinto".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198739_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa198739-310">[310]</a></sup> It was the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a> of Japanese prehistory which first left traces of material and iconography prefiguring that later included in Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200214Hardacre201718_375-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200214Hardacre201718-375">[375]</a></sup> <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span> were worshipped at various landscape features during this period; at this point, their worship consisted largely of beseeching and placating them, with little evidence that they were viewed as compassionate entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201719-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological</a> evidence suggests that <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dotaku" title="Dotaku">dotaku</a></i></span> bronze bells, bronze weapons, and metal mirrors played an important role in <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>-based ritual during the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200215Hardacre201719_376-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200215Hardacre201719-376">[376]</a></sup>
</p><p>In this early period, Japan was not a unified state; by the <a href="/wiki/Kofun_period" title="Kofun period">Kofun period</a> it was divided among <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Uji_(clan)" title="Uji (clan)">Uji</a></i></span> (clans), each with their own tutelary <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">ujigami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200215Hardacre201724_377-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200215Hardacre201724-377">[377]</a></sup> Korean migration during the Kofun period brought Confucianism and Buddhism to Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201723_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201723-378">[378]</a></sup> Buddhism had a particular impact on the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> cults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724-379">[379]</a></sup> Migrant groups and Japanese who increasingly aligned with these foreign influences built Buddhist temples in various parts of the Japanese islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724_379-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724-379">[379]</a></sup> Several rival clans who were more hostile to these foreign influences began adapting the shrines of their <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> to more closely resemble the new Buddhist structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724_379-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201724-379">[379]</a></sup> In the late 5th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamato_dynasty" title="Yamato dynasty">Yamato clan</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Y%C5%ABryaku" title="Emperor Yūryaku">Yūryaku</a> declared himself <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E3%81%A0%E3%81%84%E3%81%8A%E3%81%86" title="wikt:だいおう">daiō</a></i> ("great king") and established hegemony over much of Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201725_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201725-380">[380]</a></sup> From the early 6th century CE, the style of ritual favored by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yamato_dynasty" title="Yamato dynasty">Yamato</a> began spreading to other <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> shrines around Japan as the Yamato extended their territorial influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201727_381-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201727-381">[381]</a></sup> Buddhism was also growing. According to the <i>Nihon Shoki</i>, in 587 <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Y%C5%8Dmei" title="Emperor Yōmei">Emperor Yōmei</a> converted to Buddhism and under his sponsorship Buddhism spread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201728_382-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201728-382">[382]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_(%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1877" data-file-width="1115" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png/170px-Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png/255px-Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png/340px-Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_%28%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shinpukuji-bon_Kojiki_(%E7%9C%9F%E7%A6%8F%E5%AF%BA%E6%9C%AC%E5%8F%A4%E4%BA%8B%E8%A8%98).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from the 14th-century Shinpukuji manuscript of the <i>Kojiki</i>, itself written in the 8th century</div></div></div>
<p>In the mid-7th century, a legal code called <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ritsury%C5%8D" title="Ritsuryō">Ritsuryō</a></i></span> was adopted to establish a Chinese-style centralised government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717_383-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717-383">[383]</a></sup> As part of this, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingikan" title="Jingikan">Jingikan</a> ("Council of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Kami</i></span>") was created to conduct rites of state and coordinate provincial ritual with that in the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717–18_384-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717–18-384">[384]</a></sup> This was done according to a code of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> law called the <i>Jingiryō</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717–18_384-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201717–18-384">[384]</a></sup> itself modelled on the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Rites" title="Book of Rites">Book of Rites</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731_385-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731-385">[385]</a></sup> The Jingikan was located in the palace precincts and maintained a register of shrines and priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201733_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201733-386">[386]</a></sup> An annual calendar of state rites were introduced to help unify Japan through <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718-8">[8]</a></sup> These legally mandated rites were outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dr%C5%8D_Code" title="Yōrō Code">Yōrō Code</a> of 718,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731_385-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731-385">[385]</a></sup> and expanded in the <i>Jogan Gishiki</i> of circa 872 and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Engi_Shiki" title="Engi Shiki">Engi Shiki</a></i> of 927.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731_385-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201731-385">[385]</a></sup> Under the Jingikan, some shrines were designated as <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kansha</i></span> ("official shrines") and given specific privileges and responsibilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201733–34_387-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201733–34-387">[387]</a></sup> Hardacre saw the Jingikan as "the institutional origin of Shinto".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201718-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 8th century, the Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenmu" title="Tenmu">Tenmu</a> commissioned a compilation of the legends and genealogies of Japan's clans, resulting in the completion of the <i>Kojiki</i> in 712. Designed to legitimate the ruling dynasty, this text created a fixed version of various stories previously circulating in oral tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201747–48_388-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201747–48-388">[388]</a></sup> The <i>Kojiki</i> omits any reference to Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764_389-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764-389">[389]</a></sup> in part because it sought to ignore foreign influences and emphasise a narrative stressing indigenous elements of Japanese culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201768_390-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201768-390">[390]</a></sup> Several years later, the <i>Nihon shoki</i> was written. Unlike the <i>Kojiki</i>, this made various references to Buddhism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764_389-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764-389">[389]</a></sup> and was aimed at a foreign audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201769_391-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201769-391">[391]</a></sup> Both of these texts sought to establish the imperial clan's descent from the sun <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> Amaterasu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764_389-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764-389">[389]</a></sup> although there were many differences in the cosmogonic narrative they provided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201757–59_392-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201757–59-392">[392]</a></sup> Quickly, the <i>Nihon shoki</i> eclipsed the <i>Kojiki</i> in terms of its influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201769_391-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201769-391">[391]</a></sup> Other texts written at this time also drew on oral traditions regarding the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Kujiki" title="Kujiki">Sendari kuji hongi</a></i> for example was probably composed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mononobe" title="Mononobe">Mononobe</a> clan while the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kogoshui" title="Kogoshui">Kogoshui</a></i> was probably put together for the <a href="/wiki/Imbe_clan" title="Imbe clan">Imbe clan</a>, and in both cases they were designed to highlight the divine origins of these respective lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764–45_393-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201764–45-393">[393]</a></sup> A government order in 713 called on each region to produce <i><a href="/wiki/Fudoki" title="Fudoki">fudoki</a></i>, records of local geography, products, and stories, with the latter revealing more traditions about the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> which were present at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton200243Hardacre201766_394-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton200243Hardacre201766-394">[394]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 8th century, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship and Buddhism were thoroughly intertwined in Japanese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20138_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaliDougill20138-178">[178]</a></sup> While the emperor and court performed Buddhist rites, they also performed others to honor the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201772_395-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201772-395">[395]</a></sup> Tenmu for example appointed a virginal imperial princess to serve as the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Sai%C5%8D" title="Saiō">saiō</a></i></span>, a form of priestess, at the Ise Shrine on his behalf, a tradition continued by subsequent emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre201782–83_396-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre201782–83-396">[396]</a></sup> From the 8th century onward up until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_(era)" title="Meiji (era)">Meiji era</a>, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> were incorporated into a Buddhist cosmology in various ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819_397-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819-397">[397]</a></sup> One view is that the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> realised that like all other life-forms, they too were trapped in the cycle of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samsara" title="Samsara">samsara</a> (rebirth) and that to escape this they had to follow Buddhist teachings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819_397-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819-397">[397]</a></sup> Alternative approaches viewed the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> as benevolent entities who protected Buddhism, or that the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> were themselves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhas" title="Buddhas">Buddhas</a>, or beings who had achieved enlightenment. In this, they could be either <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Hongaku" title="Hongaku">hongaku</a></i></span>, the pure spirits of the Buddhas, or <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Honji_suijaku" title="Honji suijaku">honji suijaku</a></i></span>, transformations of the Buddhas in their attempt to help all sentient beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819_397-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuroda19819-397">[397]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nara_period">Nara period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nara period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This period hosted many changes to the country, government, and religion. The capital is moved again to <a href="/wiki/Heij%C5%8D-ky%C5%8D" title="Heijō-kyō">Heijō-kyō</a> (modern-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>), in AD 710 by <a href="/wiki/Empress_Genmei" title="Empress Genmei">Empress Genmei</a> due to the death of the emperor. This practice was necessary due to the Shinto belief in the impurity of death and the need to avoid this pollution. However, this practice of moving the capital due to "death impurity" is then abolished by the <a href="/wiki/Taih%C5%8D_Code" title="Taihō Code">Taihō Code</a> and rise in Buddhist influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapaneseReligion1985_398-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapaneseReligion1985-398">[398]</a></sup> The establishment of the imperial city in partnership with Taihō Code is important to Shinto as the office of the Shinto rites becomes more powerful in assimilating local clan shrines into the imperial fold. New shrines are built and assimilated each time the city is moved. All of the grand shrines are regulated under <a href="/wiki/Taih%C5%8D_Code" title="Taihō Code">Taihō</a> and are required to account for incomes, priests, and practices due to their national contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapaneseReligion1985_398-1"><a href="#cite_note-JapaneseReligion1985-398">[398]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meiji_era_and_the_Empire_of_Japan">Meiji era and the Empire of Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Meiji era and the Empire of Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chosen_Jingu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="2056" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Chosen_Jingu.JPG/220px-Chosen_Jingu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Chosen_Jingu.JPG/330px-Chosen_Jingu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Chosen_Jingu.JPG/440px-Chosen_Jingu.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chosen_Jingu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dsen_Jing%C5%AB" title="Chōsen Jingū">Chōsen Jingū</a> in <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, Korea, established during the Japanese occupation of the peninsula</div></div></div>
<p>Breen and Teeuwen characterise the period between 1868 and 1915, during the Meiji era, as being the "formative years" of modern Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107-9">[9]</a></sup> It is in this period that various scholars have argued that Shinto was essentially "invented".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107-9">[9]</a></sup>
Fridell argues that scholars call the period from 1868 to 1945 the "State Shinto period" because, "during these decades, Shinto elements came under a great deal of overt state influence and control as the Japanese government systematically utilized shrine worship as a major force for mobilizing imperial loyalties on behalf of modern nation-building."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-399"><a href="#cite_note-399">[399]</a></sup> However, the government had already been treating shrines as an extension of government before Meiji; see for example the <a href="/wiki/Tenp%C5%8D_Reforms" title="Tenpō Reforms">Tenpō Reforms</a>. Moreover, according to the scholar <a href="/wiki/Jason_Josephson_Storm" title="Jason Josephson Storm">Jason Ānanda Josephson</a>, It is inaccurate to describe shrines as constituting a "state religion" or a "theocracy" during this period since they had neither organization, nor doctrine, and were uninterested in conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-400"><a href="#cite_note-400">[400]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868 was fuelled by a renewal of Confucian ethics and imperial patriotism among Japan's ruling class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108_401-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108-401">[401]</a></sup> Among these reformers, Buddhism was seen as a corrupting influence that had undermined what they envisioned as Japan's original purity and greatness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108_401-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108-401">[401]</a></sup> They wanted to place a renewed emphasis on <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship as an indigenous form of ritual, an attitude that was also fuelled by anxieties about Western expansionism and fear that Christianity would take hold in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108_401-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108-401">[401]</a></sup>
</p><p>1868, all shrine priests were placed under the authority of the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jingikan" title="Jingikan">Jingikan</a>, or Council of Kami Affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107–8_402-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20107–8-402">[402]</a></sup> A project of forcible separating <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> worship from Buddhism as implemented, with Buddhist monks, deities, buildings, and rituals being banned from <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108_401-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108-401">[401]</a></sup> Buddhist imagery, scriptures, and ritual equipment were burnt, covered in excrement, or otherwise destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108_401-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20108-401">[401]</a></sup> In 1871, a new hierarchy of shrines was introduced, with imperial and national shrines at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20109_403-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20109-403">[403]</a></sup> Hereditary priesthoods were abolished and a new state-sanctioned system for appointing priests was introduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20109_403-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20109-403">[403]</a></sup>
In 1872, the Jingikan was closed and replaced with the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kyobusho&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kyobusho (page does not exist)">Kyobusho</a>, or Ministry of Edification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010_404-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010-404">[404]</a></sup> This coordinated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Promulgation_Campaign" title="Great Promulgation Campaign">a campaign</a> whereby <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kyodoshoku</i></span> ("national evangelists") were sent through the country to promote Japan's "Great Teaching," which included respect for the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> and obedience to the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010_404-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010-404">[404]</a></sup> This campaign was discontinued in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010_404-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201010-404">[404]</a></sup> In 1906, thousands of village shrines were merged so that most small communities had only a single shrine, where rites in honor of the emperor could be held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201011_405-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201011-405">[405]</a></sup> Shinto effectively became the state cult, one promoted with growing zeal in the build-up to the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201011_405-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201011-405">[405]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1882, the Meiji government designated 13 religious movements that were neither Buddhist nor Christian to be forms of "<a href="/wiki/Sect_Shinto" title="Sect Shinto">Sect Shinto</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOffner1979215_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffner1979215-36">[36]</a></sup> The number and name of the sects given this formal designation varied;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997112_406-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997112-406">[406]</a></sup> often they merged ideas with Shinto from Buddhism, Christian, Confucian, Daoist, and <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">Western esoteric</a> traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittleton2002100–101_407-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittleton2002100–101-407">[407]</a></sup> In the Meiji period, many local traditions died out and were replaced by nationally standardised practices encouraged from Tokyo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201012_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen201012-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the government sponsorship of shrines declined, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_nationalism" title="Japanese nationalism">Japanese nationalism</a> remained closely linked to the legends of foundation and emperors, as developed by the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kokugaku</i></span> scholars. In 1890, the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Rescript_on_Education" title="Imperial Rescript on Education">Imperial Rescript on Education</a> was issued, and students were required to ritually recite its oath to "offer yourselves courageously to the State" as well as to protect the Imperial family. Such processes continued to deepen throughout the early <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_era" title="Shōwa era">Shōwa era</a>, coming to an abrupt end in August 1945 when Japan lost <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">the war in the Pacific</a>. On 1 January 1946, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Sh%C5%8Dwa" title="Emperor Shōwa">Emperor Shōwa</a> issued the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ningen-sengen" title="Ningen-sengen">Ningen-sengen</a>, in which he quoted the <a href="/wiki/Charter_Oath" title="Charter Oath">Five Charter Oath</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a> and declared that he was not an <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ningen-sengen" title="Ningen-sengen">akitsumikami</a></i></span> (a deity in human form).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Diet_Library_408-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Diet_Library-408">[408]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">Post-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Post-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2726" data-file-width="2790" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg/220px-Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg/330px-Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg/440px-Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Association_of_Shinto_Shrines_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The headquarters of the Association of Shinto Shrines in <a href="/wiki/Shibuya" title="Shibuya">Shibuya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>During the U.S. occupation, a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">new Japanese constitution</a> was drawn up. This enshrined <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">separated religion from the state</a>, a measure designed to eradicate <a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUeda1979304Kitagawa1987171Bocking199718Earhart2004207_409-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUeda1979304Kitagawa1987171Bocking199718Earhart2004207-409">[409]</a></sup> As part of this, the emperor formally declared that he was not a <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207_410-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207-410">[410]</a></sup> any Shinto rituals performed by the imperial family became their own private affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUeda1979304_411-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUeda1979304-411">[411]</a></sup> This disestablishment ended government subsidies to shrines and gave them renewed freedom to organise their own affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207_410-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207-410">[410]</a></sup> In 1946 many shrines formed a voluntary organisation, the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Shinto_Shrines" title="Association of Shinto Shrines">Association of Shinto Shrines</a> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">Jinja Honchō</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199775Earhart2004207–208_412-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199775Earhart2004207–208-412">[412]</a></sup> In 1956 the association issued a creedal statement, the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">keishin seikatsu no kōryō</i></span> ("general characteristics of a life lived in reverence of the <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span>"), to summarise what they regarded as Shinto's principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199794_201-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199794-201">[201]</a></sup> By the late 1990s around 80% of Japan's Shinto shrines were part of this association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199776_413-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199776-413">[413]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the post-war decades, many Japanese blamed Shinto for encouraging the militarism which had led to defeat and occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207_410-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004207-410">[410]</a></sup> Others remained nostalgic for State Shinto,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987172_414-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987172-414">[414]</a></sup> and concerns were repeatedly expressed that sectors of Japanese society were conspiring to restore it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201118_415-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201118-415">[415]</a></sup> Various legal debates revolved around the involvement of public officials in Shinto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking199718_416-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking199718-416">[416]</a></sup> In 1965, for instance, the city of <a href="/wiki/Tsu,_Mie" title="Tsu, Mie">Tsu</a>, Mie Prefecture paid four Shinto priests to purify the site where the municipal athletic hall was to be built. Critics brought the case to court, claiming it contravened the constitutional separation of religion and state; in 1971 the high court ruled that the city administration's act had been unconstitutional, although this was overturned by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Japan" title="Supreme Court of Japan">Supreme Court</a> in 1977.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUeda1979307Breen201071–72_417-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUeda1979307Breen201071–72-417">[417]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the post-war period, Shinto themes often blended into Japanese <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movements</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson1996180_418-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson1996180-418">[418]</a></sup> of the Sect Shinto groups, <a href="/wiki/Tenrikyo" title="Tenrikyo">Tenrikyo</a> was probably the most successful in the post-war decades,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987172_414-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKitagawa1987172-414">[414]</a></sup> although in 1970 it repudiated its Shinto identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997113_419-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997113-419">[419]</a></sup> Shinto perspectives also influenced Japanese popular culture. The film director <a href="/wiki/Hayao_Miyazaki" title="Hayao Miyazaki">Hayao Miyazaki</a> of <a href="/wiki/Studio_Ghibli" title="Studio Ghibli">Studio Ghibli</a> for instance acknowledged Shinto influences on his films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Spirited_Away" title="Spirited Away">Spirited Away</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoydNishimura20163_420-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydNishimura20163-420">[420]</a></sup> Shinto also spread abroad through both Japanese migrants and conversion by non-Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xivSuga201048_421-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xivSuga201048-421">[421]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tsubaki_Grand_Shrine" title="Tsubaki Grand Shrine">Tsubaki Grand Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Suzuka,_Mie" title="Suzuka, Mie">Suzuka</a>, Mie Prefecture, was the first to establish a branch abroad: the <a href="/wiki/Tsubaki_Grand_Shrine_of_America" title="Tsubaki Grand Shrine of America">Tsubaki Grand Shrine of America</a>, initially located in California and then moved to <a href="/wiki/Granite_Falls,_Washington" title="Granite Falls, Washington">Granite Falls, Washington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken201132_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken201132-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 20th century, most academic research on Shinto was conducted by Shinto theologians, often priests,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997176_422-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997176-422">[422]</a></sup> bringing accusations that it often blurred theology with historical analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardacre20174_423-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardacre20174-423">[423]</a></sup> From the 1980s onward, there was a renewed academic interest in Shinto both in Japan and abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBocking1997177_424-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBocking1997177-424">[424]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg/220px-Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg/330px-Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg/440px-Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guji_Francesco_Brigante.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Shinto rite carried out at a jinja in <a href="/wiki/San_Marino" title="San Marino">San Marino</a>, Southern Europe</div></div></div>
<p>Most Japanese participate in several religious traditions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart20044,_214_425-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart20044,_214-425">[425]</a></sup> with Breen and Teeuwen noting that, "with few exceptions", it is not possible to differentiate between Shintoists and Buddhists in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20102_426-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20102-426">[426]</a></sup> The main exceptions are members of minority religious groups, including Christianity, which promote exclusivist worldviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004215_427-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004215-427">[427]</a></sup> Determining the proportions of the country's population who engage in Shinto activity is hindered by the fact that, if asked, Japanese people will often say "I have no religion".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004215_427-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart2004215-427">[427]</a></sup> Many Japanese avoid the term "religion", in part because they dislike the connotations of the word which most closely matches it in the Japanese language, <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shūkyō</i></span>. The latter term derives from <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">shū</i></span> ("sect") and <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kyō</i></span> ("doctrine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson19968_428-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson19968-428">[428]</a></sup>
