</p><p>Official statistics show Shinto to be Japan's largest religion, with over 80 percent of the country's population identified as engaging in Shinto activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_429-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-429">[429]</a></sup> Conversely, in questionnaires only a small minority of Japanese describe themselves as "Shintoists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101-184">[184]</a></sup> This indicates that a far larger number of people engage in Shinto activities than cite Shinto as their religious identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreenTeeuwen20101-184">[184]</a></sup> There are no formal rituals to become a practitioner of "folk Shinto". Thus, "Shinto membership" is often estimated counting only those who do join organised Shinto sects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliamsBharMarty20044–5_430-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliamsBharMarty20044–5-430">[430]</a></sup> Shinto has about 81,000 shrines and about 85,000 priests in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_429-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-429">[429]</a></sup> According to surveys carried out in 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dentsu2006_431-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dentsu2006-431">[431]</a></sup> and 2008,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHK2008_432-0"><a href="#cite_note-NHK2008-432">[432]</a></sup> less than 40% of the population of Japan identifies with an organised religion: around 35% are <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhists</a>, 30% to 40% are members of <a href="/wiki/Shinto_sects_and_schools#Shintō_inspired_religions" title="Shinto sects and schools">Shinto sects and derived religions</a>. In 2008, 26% of the participants reported often visiting Shinto shrines, while only 16.2% expressed belief in the existence of <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kami</i></span> in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NHK2008_432-1"><a href="#cite_note-NHK2008-432">[432]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_Japan">Outside Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Outside Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="1999" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg/220px-Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg/330px-Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg/440px-Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japan_310316_Tsurugaoka_Hachiman-gu_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fox statue guarding the Inari shrine at Tsurugaoka Hachiman-gū in Kamakura</div></div></div>
<p>Shinto is primarily found in Japan, although the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_colonial_empire" title="Japanese colonial empire">period of the empire</a> it was introduced to various Japanese colonies and in the present is also practiced by members of the Japanese diaspora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEarhart200431-29">[29]</a></sup>
Jinja outside Japan are termed <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja-Latn">kaigai jinja</i></span> ("overseas shrines"), a term coined by Ogasawara Shōzō.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuga201048_433-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuga201048-433">[433]</a></sup> These were established both in territories conquered by the Japanese and in areas where Japanese migrants settled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuga201048_433-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuga201048-433">[433]</a></sup> When the Japanese Empire collapsed in the 1940s, there were over 600 public shrines, and over 1,000 smaller shrines, within Japan's conquered territories. Many of these were then disbanded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuga201048_433-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuga201048-433">[433]</a></sup> Shinto has attracted interest outside of Japan, in part because it lacks the doctrinal focus of major religions found in other parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xiv_434-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xiv-434">[434]</a></sup>
Shinto was introduced to United States largely by interested <a href="/wiki/European_Americans" title="European Americans">European Americans</a> rather than by Japanese migrants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xiv_434-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPicken2011xiv-434">[434]</a></sup> Japanese migrants established several shrines in Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuga201059–60_435-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuga201059–60-435">[435]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shinto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
